<td><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio"><img alt="Ohio" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Ohio.svg/23px-Flag_of_Ohio.svg.png" width="23" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Ohio.svg/35px-Flag_of_Ohio.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Ohio.svg/46px-Flag_of_Ohio.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="320" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/23rd_Ohio_Infantry" title="23rd Ohio Infantry">23rd Ohio Infantry</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Battles/wars</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>William McKinley</b> (January 29, 1843 – September 14, 1901) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">25th</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>, serving from March 4, 1897, until <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_William_McKinley" title="Assassination of William McKinley">his assassination</a> in September 1901, six months into his second term. McKinley led the nation to victory in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a>, raised <a href="/wiki/Protective_tariff" title="Protective tariff">protective tariffs</a> to promote American industry, and maintained the nation on the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> in a rejection of <a href="/wiki/Free_silver" title="Free silver">inflationary proposals</a>.</p>
<p>McKinley was the last president to have served in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, beginning as a private in the <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a> and ending as a <a href="/wiki/Brevet_(military)" title="Brevet (military)">brevet</a> major. After the war, he settled in <a href="/wiki/Canton,_Ohio" title="Canton, Ohio">Canton, Ohio</a>, where he practiced law and married <a href="/wiki/Ida_McKinley" title="Ida McKinley" class="mw-redirect">Ida Saxton</a>. In 1876, he was elected to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a>, where he became the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>’s expert on the <a href="/wiki/Protective_tariff" title="Protective tariff">protective tariff</a>, which he promised would bring prosperity. His 1890 <a href="/wiki/McKinley_Tariff" title="McKinley Tariff">McKinley Tariff</a> was highly controversial; which together with a <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> redistricting aimed at <a href="/wiki/Gerrymandering" title="Gerrymandering">gerrymandering</a> him out of office, led to his defeat in the Democratic landslide of 1890. He was elected Ohio's governor in 1891 and 1893, steering a moderate course between capital and labor interests. With the aid of his close adviser <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hanna" title="Mark Hanna">Mark Hanna</a>, he secured the Republican nomination for president in 1896, amid a deep economic depression. He defeated his Democratic rival, <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a>, after a <a href="/wiki/Front_porch_campaign" title="Front porch campaign">front-porch campaign</a> in which he advocated "sound money" (the gold standard unless altered by international agreement) and promised that high tariffs would restore prosperity.</p>
<p>Rapid economic growth marked McKinley's presidency. He promoted the 1897 <a href="/wiki/Dingley_Tariff" title="Dingley Tariff" class="mw-redirect">Dingley Tariff</a> to protect manufacturers and factory workers from foreign competition, and in 1900, he secured the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Gold_Standard_Act" title="Gold Standard Act">Gold Standard Act</a>. McKinley hoped to persuade <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> to grant independence to rebellious <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> without conflict, but when negotiation failed, he led the nation in the Spanish–American War of 1898; the U.S. victory was quick and decisive. As part of <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1898)" title="Treaty of Paris (1898)">the peace settlement</a>, Spain turned over to the United States its main overseas colonies of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>; Cuba was promised independence, but at that time remained under the control of the U.S. Army. The United States <a href="/wiki/Hawaii#Annexation.E2.80.94the_Territory_of_Hawaii_.281898.E2.80.931959.29" title="Hawaii">annexed</a> the independent <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Hawaii" title="Republic of Hawaii">Republic of Hawaii</a> in 1898 and it became a U.S. <a href="/wiki/Territory_of_Hawaii" title="Territory of Hawaii">territory</a>.</p>
<p>Historians regard McKinley's 1896 victory as a <a href="/wiki/Realigning_election" title="Realigning election">realigning election</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Third_Party_System" title="Third Party System">the political stalemate</a> of the post-Civil War era gave way to the Republican-dominated <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Party_System" title="Fourth Party System">Fourth Party System</a>, which began with the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">Progressive Era</a>. McKinley defeated Bryan again in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1900" title="United States presidential election, 1900">1900 presidential election</a>, in a campaign focused on <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionism</a>, and free silver. However, his legacy was quickly cut short when he was <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_William_McKinley" title="Assassination of William McKinley">assassinated</a> on September 6, 1901, by <a href="/wiki/Leon_Czolgosz" title="Leon Czolgosz">Leon Czolgosz</a>, a second-generation <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Polish</a>-American with <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_in_the_United_States" title="Anarchism in the United States">anarchist</a> leanings; McKinley was succeeded by Vice President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>. As an innovator of American <a href="/wiki/Interventionism_(politics)" title="Interventionism (politics)">interventionism</a> and pro-business sentiment, McKinley's presidency is <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">generally considered above average</a>, though his universally positive public perception was soon overshadowed by Roosevelt.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family">Early life and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_boy.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/McKinley_boy.png/170px-McKinley_boy.png" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/McKinley_boy.png/255px-McKinley_boy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f1/McKinley_boy.png 2x" data-file-width="340" data-file-height="411" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_boy.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
William McKinley at age 15</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>William McKinley Jr., was born in 1843 in <a href="/wiki/Niles,_Ohio" title="Niles, Ohio">Niles, Ohio</a>, the seventh child of <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley_Sr." title="William McKinley Sr." class="mw-redirect">William</a> and Nancy (née Allison) McKinley.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech4Morgan2_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech4Morgan2-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> The McKinleys were of <a href="/wiki/English_American" title="English American">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotch-Irish_American" title="Scotch-Irish American">Scots-Irish</a> descent and had settled in western Pennsylvania in the 18th century. There, the elder McKinley was born in <a href="/wiki/Pine_Township,_Mercer_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Pine Township, Mercer County, Pennsylvania">Pine Township</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech4Morgan2_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech4Morgan2-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The family moved to Ohio when the senior McKinley was a boy, settling in <a href="/wiki/Lisbon,_Ohio" title="Lisbon, Ohio">New Lisbon</a> (now Lisbon). He met Nancy Allison there, and married her later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech4Morgan2_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech4Morgan2-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> The Allison family was of mostly English blood and among Pennsylvania’s earliest settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan3_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan3-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> The family trade on both sides was iron-making, and McKinley senior operated <a href="/wiki/Foundry" title="Foundry">foundries</a> throughout Ohio, in New Lisbon, Niles, <a href="/wiki/Poland,_Ohio" title="Poland, Ohio">Poland</a>, and finally <a href="/wiki/Canton,_Ohio" title="Canton, Ohio">Canton</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong4.E2.80.936Morgan2.E2.80.933Phillips13_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong4.E2.80.936Morgan2.E2.80.933Phillips13-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> The McKinley household was, like many from Ohio's <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Western_Reserve" title="Connecticut Western Reserve">Western Reserve</a>, steeped in <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whiggish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionist</a> sentiment, the latter based on the family's staunch <a href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist" class="mw-redirect">Methodist</a> beliefs. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips17.E2.80.9318Armstrong8Morgan10.E2.80.9311_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips17.E2.80.9318Armstrong8Morgan10.E2.80.9311-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> William followed in the Methodist tradition, becoming active in the local Methodist church at the age of sixteen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips16Leech4.E2.80.935_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips16Leech4.E2.80.935-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He was a lifelong pious Methodist.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan9.E2.80.9310_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan9.E2.80.9310-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1852, the family moved from Niles to Poland, Ohio so that their children could attend the better school there. Graduating in 1859, he enrolled the following year at <a href="/wiki/Allegheny_College" title="Allegheny College">Allegheny College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Meadville,_Pennsylvania" title="Meadville, Pennsylvania">Meadville, Pennsylvania</a>. He remained at Allegheny for only one year, returning home in 1860 after becoming ill and depressed. He also spent time at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Union_College" title="Mount Union College" class="mw-redirect">Mount Union College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alliance" title="Alliance">Alliance</a>, Ohio where he joined <a href="/wiki/Sigma_Alpha_Epsilon" title="Sigma Alpha Epsilon">Sigma Alpha Epsilon</a>. He did not graduate from either university. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips20Armstrong5_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips20Armstrong5-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Although his health recovered, family finances declined and McKinley was unable to return to Allegheny, first working as a postal clerk and later taking a job teaching at a school near Poland, Ohio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong6Morgan11.E2.80.9312_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong6Morgan11.E2.80.9312-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War">Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Virginia_and_Antietam">Western Virginia and Antietam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Western Virginia and Antietam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:General_Hayes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/General_Hayes.jpg/170px-General_Hayes.jpg" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/General_Hayes.jpg/255px-General_Hayes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/General_Hayes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="269" data-file-height="354" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:General_Hayes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">Rutherford B. Hayes</a> was McKinley's mentor during the Civil War and afterward.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When the Southern states seceded from the Union and the American Civil War began, thousands of men in Ohio volunteered for service.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong1_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong1-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Among them were McKinley and his cousin William McKinley Osbourne, who enlisted as <a href="/wiki/Private_(rank)" title="Private (rank)">privates</a> in the newly formed Poland Guards in July 1861.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong3.E2.80.934Phillips20.E2.80.9321_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong3.E2.80.934Phillips20.E2.80.9321-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> The men left for <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Ohio" title="Columbus, Ohio">Columbus</a> where they were consolidated with other small units to form the <a href="/wiki/23rd_Ohio_Infantry" title="23rd Ohio Infantry">23rd Ohio Infantry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong8.E2.80.9310_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong8.E2.80.9310-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> The men were unhappy to learn that, unlike Ohio’s earlier volunteer <a href="/wiki/Regiment" title="Regiment">regiments</a>, they would not be permitted to elect their officers; they would be designated by Ohio’s governor, <a href="/wiki/William_Dennison_Jr." title="William Dennison Jr.">William Dennison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong8.E2.80.9310_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong8.E2.80.9310-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Dennison appointed <a href="/wiki/Colonel_(United_States)" title="Colonel (United States)">Colonel</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Rosecrans" title="William Rosecrans">William Rosecrans</a> as the commander of the regiment, and the men began training on the outskirts of Columbus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong8.E2.80.9310_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong8.E2.80.9310-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley quickly took to the soldier’s life and wrote a series of letters to his hometown newspaper extolling <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">the army</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union cause</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong10.E2.80.9311_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong10.E2.80.9311-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Delays in issuance of uniforms and weapons again brought the men into conflict with their officers, but <a href="/wiki/Major_(United_States)" title="Major (United States)">Major</a> <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">Rutherford B. Hayes</a> convinced them to accept what the government had issued them; his style in dealing with the men impressed McKinley, beginning an association and friendship that would last until Hayes’ death in 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong12.E2.80.9314_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong12.E2.80.9314-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After a month of training, McKinley and the 23rd Ohio, now led by Colonel <a href="/wiki/Eliakim_P._Scammon" title="Eliakim P. Scammon">Eliakim P. Scammon</a>, set out for western Virginia (today part of West Virginia) in June 1861 as a part of the <a href="/wiki/Kanawha_Division" title="Kanawha Division">Kanawha Division</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom120.E2.80.93121Armstrong14_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom120.E2.80.93121Armstrong14-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley initially thought Scammon was a <a href="/wiki/Martinet" title="Martinet">martinet</a>, but when the regiment finally saw battle, he came to appreciate the value of their relentless drilling.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong15.E2.80.9316_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong15.E2.80.9316-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Their first contact with the enemy came in September when they drove back Confederate troops at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carnifex_Ferry" title="Battle of Carnifex Ferry">Carnifex Ferry</a> in present-day West Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom125.E2.80.93126Armstrong18.E2.80.9322_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom125.E2.80.93126Armstrong18.E2.80.9322-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Three days after the battle, McKinley was assigned to duty in the <a href="/wiki/Brigade" title="Brigade">brigade</a> <a href="/wiki/Quartermaster_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Quartermaster Corps (United States Army)">quartermaster</a> office, where he worked both to supply his regiment, and as a clerk.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong22.E2.80.9323_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong22.E2.80.9323-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> In November, the regiment established winter quarters near <a href="/wiki/Fayetteville,_West_Virginia" title="Fayetteville, West Virginia">Fayetteville</a> (today in West Virginia).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom128.E2.80.93130Armstrong24.E2.80.9325_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom128.E2.80.93130Armstrong24.E2.80.9325-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley spent the winter substituting for a <a href="/wiki/Commissary" title="Commissary">commissary</a> <a href="/wiki/Sergeant" title="Sergeant">sergeant</a> who was ill, and in April 1862 he was promoted to that rank.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong25.E2.80.9329Phillips21_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong25.E2.80.9329Phillips21-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> The regiment resumed its advance that spring with Hayes in command (Scammon by then led the brigade) and fought several minor engagements against the rebel forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom136.E2.80.93141Armstrong30.E2.80.9333_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom136.E2.80.93141Armstrong30.E2.80.9333-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>That September, McKinley’s regiment was called east to reinforce General <a href="/wiki/John_Pope_(military_officer)" title="John Pope (military officer)">John Pope</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Virginia" title="Army of Virginia">Army of Virginia</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Bull_Run" title="Second Battle of Bull Run">Second Battle of Bull Run</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom141.E2.80.93143Armstrong33.E2.80.9336_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom141.E2.80.93143Armstrong33.E2.80.9336-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Delayed in passing through Washington, D.C., the 23rd Ohio did not arrive in time for the battle, but joined the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Potomac" title="Army of the Potomac">Army of the Potomac</a> as it hurried north to cut off <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Northern_Virginia" title="Army of Northern Virginia">Army of Northern Virginia</a> as it advanced into Maryland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom141.E2.80.93143Armstrong33.E2.80.9336_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom141.E2.80.93143Armstrong33.E2.80.9336-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> The 23rd was the first regiment to encounter the Confederates at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_South_Mountain" title="Battle of South Mountain">Battle of South Mountain</a> on September 14.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom146.E2.80.93148Armstrong36.E2.80.9338_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom146.E2.80.93148Armstrong36.E2.80.9338-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> After severe losses, Union forces drove back the Confederates and continued to <a href="/wiki/Sharpsburg,_Maryland" title="Sharpsburg, Maryland">Sharpsburg</a>, Maryland, where they engaged Lee’s army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Antietam" title="Battle of Antietam">Battle of Antietam</a>, one of the bloodiest battles of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong38.E2.80.9341Phillips21_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong38.E2.80.9341Phillips21-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> The 23rd was also in the thick of the fighting at Antietam, and McKinley himself came under heavy fire when bringing rations to the men on the line.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong38.E2.80.9341Phillips21_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong38.E2.80.9341Phillips21-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Medal_of_Honor_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medal_of_Honor-24"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley’s regiment again suffered many casualties, but the Army of the Potomac was victorious and the Confederates retreated into Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong38.E2.80.9341Phillips21_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong38.E2.80.9341Phillips21-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> The regiment was then detached from the Army of the Potomac and returned by train to western Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong43.E2.80.9344_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong43.E2.80.9344-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shenandoah_Valley_and_promotion">Shenandoah Valley and promotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Shenandoah Valley and promotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinleyBrady_1865.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/McKinleyBrady_1865.png/170px-McKinleyBrady_1865.png" width="170" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/McKinleyBrady_1865.png/255px-McKinleyBrady_1865.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/McKinleyBrady_1865.png/340px-McKinleyBrady_1865.png 2x" data-file-width="861" data-file-height="1238" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinleyBrady_1865.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
McKinley in 1865, just after the war. Photograph by <a href="/wiki/Mathew_Brady" title="Mathew Brady">Mathew Brady</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the regiment went into winter quarters near <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_West_Virginia" title="Charleston, West Virginia">Charleston, Virginia</a> (present-day West Virginia), McKinley was ordered back to Ohio with some other sergeants to recruit fresh troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong44.E2.80.9345_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong44.E2.80.9345-26"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> When they arrived in Columbus, Governor <a href="/wiki/David_Tod" title="David Tod">David Tod</a> surprised McKinley with a commission as <a href="/wiki/Second_lieutenant" title="Second lieutenant">second lieutenant</a> in recognition of his service at Antietam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong44.E2.80.9345_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong44.E2.80.9345-26"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley and his comrades saw little action until July 1863, when the division skirmished with <a href="/wiki/John_Hunt_Morgan" title="John Hunt Morgan">John Hunt Morgan</a>’s cavalry at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Buffington_Island" title="Battle of Buffington Island">Battle of Buffington Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom157.E2.80.93158Armstrong47.E2.80.9355_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom157.E2.80.93158Armstrong47.E2.80.9355-27"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Early in 1864, the Army command structure in West Virginia was reorganized, and the division was assigned to <a href="/wiki/George_Crook" title="George Crook">George Crook</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Army_of_West_Virginia" title="Army of West Virginia">Army of West Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom162.E2.80.93164Armstrong63.E2.80.9365_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom162.E2.80.93164Armstrong63.E2.80.9365-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> They soon resumed the offensive, marching into southwestern Virginia to destroy salt and lead mines used by the enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom162.E2.80.93164Armstrong63.E2.80.9365_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom162.E2.80.93164Armstrong63.E2.80.9365-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> On May 9, the army engaged Confederate troops at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cloyd%27s_Mountain" title="Battle of Cloyd's Mountain">Cloyd’s Mountain</a>, where the men charged the enemy entrenchments and drove the rebels from the field.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom162.E2.80.93164Armstrong63.E2.80.9365_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom162.E2.80.93164Armstrong63.E2.80.9365-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley later said the combat there was “as desperate as any witnessed during the war.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom162.E2.80.93164Armstrong63.E2.80.9365_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom162.E2.80.93164Armstrong63.E2.80.9365-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the rout, the Union forces destroyed Confederate supplies and skirmished with the enemy again successfully.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom162.E2.80.93164Armstrong63.E2.80.9365_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom162.E2.80.93164Armstrong63.E2.80.9365-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>McKinley and his regiment moved to the <a href="/wiki/Shenandoah_Valley" title="Shenandoah Valley">Shenandoah Valley</a> as the armies broke from winter quarters to <a href="/wiki/Valley_Campaigns_of_1864" title="Valley Campaigns of 1864">resume hostilities</a>. Crook’s corps was attached to <a href="/wiki/Major_general_(United_States)" title="Major general (United States)">Major General</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Hunter" title="David Hunter">David Hunter</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Union_Army_of_the_Shenandoah" title="Union Army of the Shenandoah">Army of the Shenandoah</a> and soon back in contact with Confederate forces, capturing <a href="/wiki/Lexington,_Virginia" title="Lexington, Virginia">Lexington, Virginia</a>, on June 11.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom166.E2.80.93168Armstrong66.E2.80.9369_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom166.E2.80.93168Armstrong66.E2.80.9369-29"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> They continued south toward <a href="/wiki/Lynchburg,_Virginia" title="Lynchburg, Virginia">Lynchburg</a>, tearing up railroad track as they advanced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom166.E2.80.93168Armstrong66.E2.80.9369_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom166.E2.80.93168Armstrong66.E2.80.9369-29"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Hunter believed the troops at Lynchburg were too powerful, however, and the brigade returned to West Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom166.E2.80.93168Armstrong66.E2.80.9369_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom166.E2.80.93168Armstrong66.E2.80.9369-29"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Before the army could make another attempt, Confederate General <a href="/wiki/Jubal_Early" title="Jubal Early">Jubal Early</a>’s raid into Maryland forced their recall to the north.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong70.E2.80.9371_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong70.E2.80.9371-30"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Early’s army surprised them at <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Kernstown" title="Second Battle of Kernstown">Kernstown</a> on July 24, where McKinley came under heavy fire and the army was defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom168.E2.80.93169Armstrong72.E2.80.9373_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom168.E2.80.93169Armstrong72.E2.80.9373-31"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Retreating into Maryland, the army was reorganized again: Major General <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sheridan" title="Philip Sheridan">Philip Sheridan</a> replaced Hunter, and McKinley, who had been promoted to <a href="/wiki/Captain_(United_States_O-3)" title="Captain (United States O-3)">captain</a> after the battle, was transferred to General Crook’s staff.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom170.E2.80.93171Armstrong75.E2.80.9377_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom170.E2.80.93171Armstrong75.E2.80.9377-32"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> By August, Early was retreating south in the valley, with Sheridan’s army in pursuit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong78.E2.80.9380_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong78.E2.80.9380-33"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> They fended off a Confederate assault at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Berryville" title="Battle of Berryville">Berryville</a>, where McKinley had a horse shot out from under him, and advanced to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Opequon" title="Battle of Opequon" class="mw-redirect">Opequon Creek</a>, where they broke the enemy lines and pursued them farther south.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom172.E2.80.93173Armstrong80.E2.80.9382_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom172.E2.80.93173Armstrong80.E2.80.9382-34"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> They followed up the victory with another at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fisher%27s_Hill" title="Battle of Fisher's Hill">Fisher’s Hill</a> on September 22, and were engaged once more at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cedar_Creek" title="Battle of Cedar Creek">Cedar Creek</a> on October 19.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong84.E2.80.9391_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong84.E2.80.9391-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> After initially falling back from the Confederate advance, McKinley helped to rally the troops and turn the tide of the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong84.E2.80.9391_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong84.E2.80.9391-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After Cedar Creek, the army stayed in the vicinity through election day, when McKinley cast his first presidential ballot, for the incumbent Republican, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong84.E2.80.9391_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong84.E2.80.9391-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> The next day, they moved north up the valley into winter quarters near Kernstown.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong84.E2.80.9391_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong84.E2.80.9391-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> In February 1865, Crook was captured by Confederate raiders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong95.E2.80.9396_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong95.E2.80.9396-36"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Crook's capture added to the confusion as the army was reorganized for the spring campaign, and McKinley found himself serving on the staffs of four different generals over the next fifteen days — Crook, <a href="/wiki/John_Dunlap_Stevenson" title="John Dunlap Stevenson">John D. Stevenson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_S._Carroll" title="Samuel S. Carroll">Samuel S. Carroll</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott_Hancock" title="Winfield Scott Hancock">Winfield S. Hancock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong95.E2.80.9396_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong95.E2.80.9396-36"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Finally assigned to Carroll’s staff again, McKinley acted as the general’s first and only <a href="/wiki/Adjutant" title="Adjutant">adjutant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong98.E2.80.9399_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong98.E2.80.9399-37"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Lee and his army <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Appomattox_Court_House#Surrender" title="Battle of Appomattox Court House">surrendered to</a> <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_general_(United_States)" title="Lieutenant general (United States)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a> a few days later, effectively ending the war. McKinley found time to join a <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemason</a> lodge (later renamed after him) in <a href="/wiki/Winchester,_Virginia" title="Winchester, Virginia">Winchester, Virginia</a>, before he and Carroll were transferred to Hancock’s First Veterans Corps in Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong99.E2.80.93101_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong99.E2.80.93101-38"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Just before the war’s end, McKinley received his final promotion, a <a href="/wiki/Brevet_(military)" title="Brevet (military)">brevet</a> commission as major.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong103.E2.80.93105_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong103.E2.80.93105-39"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> In July, the Veterans Corps was mustered out of service, and McKinley and Carroll were relieved of their duties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong103.E2.80.93105_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong103.E2.80.93105-39"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Carroll and Hancock encouraged McKinley to apply for a place in the peacetime army, but he declined and returned to Ohio the following month.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong103.E2.80.93105_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong103.E2.80.93105-39"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_career_and_marriage">Legal career and marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Legal career and marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISMcKinley.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/ISMcKinley.jpg/170px-ISMcKinley.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/ISMcKinley.jpg/255px-ISMcKinley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/ISMcKinley.jpg/340px-ISMcKinley.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3035" data-file-height="3780" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISMcKinley.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ida Saxton McKinley</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the war ended in 1865, McKinley decided on a career in the law and began <a href="/wiki/Reading_law" title="Reading law">studying</a> in the office of an attorney in <a href="/wiki/Poland,_Ohio" title="Poland, Ohio">Poland, Ohio</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan28.E2.80.9330_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan28.E2.80.9330-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> The following year, he continued his studies by attending <a href="/wiki/Albany_Law_School" title="Albany Law School">Albany Law School</a> in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan30.E2.80.9331_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan30.E2.80.9331-41"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> After studying there for less than a year, McKinley returned home and was admitted to the <a href="/wiki/Bar_(law)" title="Bar (law)">bar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Warren,_Ohio" title="Warren, Ohio">Warren, Ohio</a>, in March 1867.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan30.E2.80.9331_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan30.E2.80.9331-41"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> That same year, he moved to Canton, the county seat of <a href="/wiki/Stark_County,_Ohio" title="Stark County, Ohio">Stark County</a>, and set up a small office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan30.E2.80.9331_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan30.E2.80.9331-41"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> He soon formed a partnership with <a href="/w/index.php?title=George_W._Belden&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="George W. Belden (page does not exist)">George W. Belden</a>, an experienced lawyer and former judge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan31.E2.80.9333Leech12.2C_21_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan31.E2.80.9333Leech12.2C_21-42"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> His practice was successful enough for him to buy a block of buildings on Main Street in Canton, which provided him with a small but consistent rental income for decades to come.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan31.E2.80.9333Leech12.2C_21_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan31.E2.80.9333Leech12.2C_21-42"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> When his Army friend <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">Rutherford B. Hayes</a> was nominated for governor in 1867, McKinley made speeches on his behalf in Stark County, his first foray into politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech11.E2.80.9312_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech11.E2.80.9312-43"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> The county was closely divided between <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republicans</a>, but Hayes carried it that year in his statewide victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech11.E2.80.9312_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech11.E2.80.9312-43"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1869, McKinley ran for the office of <a href="/wiki/District_attorney" title="District attorney">prosecuting attorney</a> of Stark County, an office usually then held by Democrats, and was unexpectedly elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan34.E2.80.9335_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan34.E2.80.9335-44"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> When McKinley ran for re-election in 1871, the Democrats nominated <a href="/wiki/William_A._Lynch" title="William A. Lynch">William A. Lynch</a>, a prominent local lawyer, and McKinley was defeated by 143 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan34.E2.80.9335_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan34.E2.80.9335-44"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As McKinley’s professional career progressed, so too did his social life blossom as he wooed <a href="/wiki/Ida_Saxton_McKinley" title="Ida Saxton McKinley">Ida Saxton</a>, the daughter of a prominent Canton family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan34.E2.80.9335_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan34.E2.80.9335-44"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> They were married on January 25, 1871, in the newly built First Presbyterian Church of Canton, although Ida soon joined her husband's Methodist church.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Their first child, <a href="/wiki/Katherine_McKinley" title="Katherine McKinley">Katherine</a>, was born on Christmas Day 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> A second daughter, Ida, followed in 1873, but died the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley’s wife descended into a deep depression at her baby’s death and her health, never robust, grew worse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Two years later, in 1875, Katherine died of <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a>. Ida never recovered from her daughters’ deaths; the McKinleys had no more children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Ida McKinley developed <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a> around the same time and thereafter disliked her husband's leaving her side.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320_45-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> He remained a devoted husband and tended to his wife’s medical and emotional needs for the rest of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320_45-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan37.E2.80.9339Leech16.E2.80.9320-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Ida insisted that McKinley continue his increasingly successful career in law and politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan39.E2.80.9340_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan39.E2.80.9340-46"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> He attended the state Republican convention that nominated Hayes for a third term as governor in 1875, and campaigned again for his old friend in the election that fall.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan39.E2.80.9340_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan39.E2.80.9340-46"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> The next year, McKinley undertook a high-profile case defending a group of <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">coal miners</a> arrested for rioting after a clash with <a href="/wiki/Strikebreaker" title="Strikebreaker">strikebreakers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan40.E2.80.9341Weisenburger78.E2.80.9380_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan40.E2.80.9341Weisenburger78.E2.80.9380-47"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Lynch, McKinley's opponent in the 1871 election, and his partner, <a href="/wiki/William_R._Day" title="William R. Day">William R. Day</a>, were the opposing counsel, and the mine owners included <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hanna" title="Mark Hanna">Mark Hanna</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a> businessman.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan40.E2.80.9341Weisenburger78.E2.80.9380_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan40.E2.80.9341Weisenburger78.E2.80.9380-47"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Taking the case <i><a href="/wiki/Pro_bono" title="Pro bono">pro bono</a>,</i> he was successful in getting all but one of the miners acquitted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan40.E2.80.9341Weisenburger78.E2.80.9380_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan40.E2.80.9341Weisenburger78.E2.80.9380-47"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> The case raised McKinley’s standing among laborers, a crucial part of the Stark County electorate, and also introduced him to Hanna, who would become his strongest backer in years to come.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan40.E2.80.9341Weisenburger78.E2.80.9380_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan40.E2.80.9341Weisenburger78.E2.80.9380-47"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>McKinley’s good standing with labor became useful that year as he campaigned for the Republican nomination for <a href="/wiki/Ohio%27s_17th_congressional_district" title="Ohio's 17th congressional district">Ohio's 17th congressional district</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan42_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan42-48"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Delegates to the county conventions thought he could attract <a href="/wiki/Blue-collar_worker" title="Blue-collar worker">blue-collar</a> voters, and in August 1876, McKinley was nominated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan42_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan42-48"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> By that time, Hayes had been nominated for president, and McKinley campaigned for him while running his own congressional campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan43_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan43-49"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Both were successful. McKinley, campaigning mostly on his support for a <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">protective tariff</a>, defeated the Democratic nominee, <a href="/wiki/Levi_L._Lamborn" title="Levi L. Lamborn">Levi L. Lamborn</a>, by 3,300 votes, while Hayes won <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1876" title="United States presidential election, 1876">a hotly disputed election</a> to reach the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan43_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan43-49"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley’s victory came at a personal cost: his income as a congressman would be half of what he earned as a lawyer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcElroy31_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcElroy31-50"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rising_politician_1877.E2.80.931895">Rising politician 1877–1895</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Rising politician 1877–1895">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spokesman_for_protection">Spokesman for protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Spokesman for protection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mckin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Mckin.jpg/170px-Mckin.jpg" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Mckin.jpg/255px-Mckin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Mckin.jpg/340px-Mckin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3800" data-file-height="4880" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mckin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Congressman McKinley</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote">For additional information on the currency question, see <a href="/wiki/Cross_of_Gold_speech#Background" title="Cross of Gold speech">Cross of Gold speech#Background</a>.</div>
<blockquote>
<p>"Under <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> the trader is the master and the producer the slave. Protection is but the law of nature, the law of self-preservation, of self-development, of securing the highest and best destiny of the race of man. [It is said] that protection is immoral.... Why, if protection builds up and elevates 63,000,000 [the U.S. population] of people, the influence of those 63,000,000 of people elevates the rest of the world. We cannot take a step in the pathway of progress without benefiting mankind everywhere. Well, they say, 'Buy where you can buy the cheapest'.... Of course, that applies to labor as to everything else. Let me give you a maxim that is a thousand times better than that, and it is the protection maxim: 'Buy where you can pay the easiest.' And that spot of earth is where labor wins its highest rewards."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>McKinley first took his congressional seat in October 1877, when <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">President Hayes</a> summoned Congress into special session.<sup id="cite_ref-Twentieth_Amendment_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Twentieth_Amendment-52"><span>[</span>b<span>]</span></a></sup> With the Republicans in the minority, McKinley was given unimportant committee assignments, which he undertook conscientiously.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech20_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech20-53"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley’s friendship with Hayes did McKinley little good on <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Hill" title="Capitol Hill">Capitol Hill</a>; the President was not well-regarded by many leaders there.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech37_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech37-54"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> The young congressman broke with Hayes on the question of the currency, but it did not affect their friendship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan47_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan47-55"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The United States had effectively been placed on the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1873" title="Coinage Act of 1873">Coinage Act of 1873</a>; when silver prices dropped significantly, many sought to make silver again a legal tender, equally with gold. Such a course would be inflationary, but advocates argued that the economic benefits of the increased <a href="/wiki/Money_supply" title="Money supply">money supply</a> would be worth the inflation; opponents warned that “<a href="/wiki/Free_silver" title="Free silver">free silver</a>” would not bring the promised benefits and would harm the United States in international trade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner180.E2.80.93181_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner180.E2.80.93181-56"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley voted for the <a href="/wiki/Bland-Allison_Act" title="Bland-Allison Act" class="mw-redirect">Bland-Allison Act</a> of 1878, which mandated large government purchases of silver for striking into money, and also joined the large majorities in each house that overrode Hayes’ veto of the legislation. In so doing, McKinley voted against the position of the House Republican leader, his fellow Ohioan and friend, <a href="/wiki/James_Garfield" title="James Garfield" class="mw-redirect">James Garfield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan46.E2.80.9347Horner181.E2.80.93182_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan46.E2.80.9347Horner181.E2.80.93182-57"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>From his first term in Congress, McKinley was a strong advocate of protective tariffs. The primary purposes of such imposts was not to raise revenue, but to allow American manufacturing to develop by giving it a price advantage in the domestic market over foreign competitors. McKinley biographer <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Leech" title="Margaret Leech">Margaret Leech</a> noted that Canton had become prosperous as a center for the manufacture of farm equipment because of <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protection</a>, and that this may have helped form his political views. McKinley introduced and supported bills that raised protective tariffs, and opposed those that lowered them or imposed tariffs simply to raise revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech36.E2.80.9337Phillips42.E2.80.9344_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech36.E2.80.9337Phillips42.E2.80.9344-58"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield’s election as president in 1880 created a vacancy on the <a href="/wiki/House_Ways_and_Means_Committee" title="House Ways and Means Committee" class="mw-redirect">House Ways and Means Committee</a>; McKinley was selected to fill it, placing him on the most powerful committee after only two terms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan55_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan55-59"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>McKinley increasingly became a significant figure in national politics. In 1880, he served a brief term as Ohio’s representative on the <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a>. In 1884, he was elected a delegate to <a href="/wiki/1884_Republican_National_Convention" title="1884 Republican National Convention">that year’s Republican convention</a>, where he served as chair of the Committee on Resolutions and won plaudits for his handling of the convention when called upon to preside. By 1886, McKinley, Senator <a href="/wiki/John_Sherman" title="John Sherman">John Sherman</a>, and Governor <a href="/wiki/Joseph_B._Foraker" title="Joseph B. Foraker">Joseph B. Foraker</a> were considered the leaders of the Republican party in Ohio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips60.E2.80.9361_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips60.E2.80.9361-60"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Sherman, who had helped to found the Republican Party, ran three times for the Republican nomination for president in the 1880s, each time failing,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan73.E2.80.9374_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan73.E2.80.9374-61"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> while Foraker began a meteoric rise in Ohio politics early in the decade. Hanna, once he entered public affairs as a political manager and generous contributor, supported Sherman’s ambitions, as well as those of Foraker. The latter relationship broke off at the <a href="/wiki/1888_Republican_National_Convention" title="1888 Republican National Convention">1888 Republican National Convention</a>, where McKinley, Foraker, and Hanna were all delegates supporting Sherman. Convinced Sherman could not win, Foraker threw his support to the unsuccessful Republican 1884 presidential nominee, <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/James_G._Blaine" title="James G. Blaine">James G. Blaine</a>. When Blaine stated he was not a candidate, Foraker returned to Sherman, but the nomination went to former <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> senator <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">Benjamin Harrison</a>, who was elected president. In the bitterness that followed the convention, Hanna abandoned Foraker, and for the rest of McKinley’s life, the Ohio Republican Party was divided into two factions, one aligned with McKinley, Sherman, and Hanna and the other with Foraker.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner59.E2.80.9360.2C_72.E2.80.9378_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner59.E2.80.9360.2C_72.E2.80.9378-62"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Hanna came to admire McKinley and became a friend and close adviser to him. Although Hanna remained active in business and in promoting other Republicans, in the years after 1888, he spent an increasing amount of time boosting McKinley’s political career.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner80.E2.80.9381_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner80.E2.80.9381-63"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1889, with the Republicans in the majority, McKinley sought election as <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker of the House</a>. He failed to gain the post, which went to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Brackett_Reed" title="Thomas Brackett Reed">Thomas B. Reed</a> of <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>; however, Speaker Reed appointed McKinley chairman of the Ways and Means Committee. The Ohioan guided the <a href="/wiki/McKinley_Tariff" title="McKinley Tariff">McKinley Tariff</a> of 1890 through Congress; although McKinley’s work was altered through the influence of special interests in the Senate, it imposed a number of protective tariffs on foreign goods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips27.2C_42.E2.80.9343_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips27.2C_42.E2.80.9343-64"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gerrymandering_and_defeat_for_re-election">Gerrymandering and defeat for re-election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Gerrymandering and defeat for re-election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recognizing McKinley’s potential, the Democrats, whenever they controlled the Ohio legislature, sought to <a href="/wiki/Gerrymandering" title="Gerrymandering">gerrymander</a> or redistrict him out of office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips27_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips27-65"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1878, McKinley faced election in a redrawn 17th district; he won anyway, causing Hayes to exult, “Oh, the good luck of McKinley! He was gerrymandered out and then beat the gerrymander! We enjoyed it as much as he did.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan54_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan54-66"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> After the 1882 election, McKinley was unseated on an election contest by a near party-line House vote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan59.E2.80.9360_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan59.E2.80.9360-67"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Out of office, he was briefly depressed by the setback, but soon vowed to run again. The Democrats again redistricted Stark County for the 1884 election; McKinley was returned to Congress anyway.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan60.E2.80.9362_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan60.E2.80.9362-68"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Off_to_Ohio.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Off_to_Ohio.png/220px-Off_to_Ohio.png" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Off_to_Ohio.png/330px-Off_to_Ohio.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Off_to_Ohio.png/440px-Off_to_Ohio.png 2x" data-file-width="638" data-file-height="902" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Off_to_Ohio.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Judge</i> magazine cover from September 1890, showing McKinley (left) having helped dispatch Speaker Reed's opponent in early-voting Maine, hurrying off with the victor to McKinley's “gerrymandered” Ohio district.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For 1890, the Democrats gerrymandered McKinley one final time, placing Stark County in the same district as one of the strongest pro-Democrat counties, <a href="/wiki/Holmes_County,_Ohio" title="Holmes County, Ohio">Holmes</a>, populated by solidly Democratic <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Dutch" title="Pennsylvania Dutch">Pennsylvania Dutch</a>. The new boundaries seemed good, based on past results, for a Democratic majority of 2000 to 3000. The Republicans could not reverse the gerrymander as legislative elections would not be held until 1891, but they could throw all their energies into the district, as the McKinley Tariff was a main theme of the Democratic campaign nationwide, and there was considerable attention paid to McKinley’s race. The Republican Party sent its leading orators to Canton, including Blaine (then <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>), Speaker Reed and President Harrison. The Democrats countered with their best spokesmen on tariff issues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJensen150.E2.80.93151_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJensen150.E2.80.93151-69"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley tirelessly stumped his new district, reaching out to its 40,000 voters to explain that his tariff</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>was framed for the people&#160;... as a defense to their industries, as a protection to the labor of their hands, as a safeguard to the happy homes of American workingmen, and as a security to their education, their wages, and their investments&#160;... It will bring to this country a prosperity unparalleled in our own history and unrivalled in the history of the world.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKinley464_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKinley464-70"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Democrats ran a strong candidate in former lieutenant governor <a href="/wiki/John_G._Warwick" title="John G. Warwick">John G. Warwick</a>. To drive their point home, they hired young partisans to pretend to be peddlers, who went door to door offering 25-cent tinware to housewives for 50 cents, explaining the rise in prices was due to the McKinley Tariff. In the end, McKinley lost by 300 votes, but the Republicans won a statewide majority and claimed a moral victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJensen151.E2.80.93153_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJensen151.E2.80.93153-71"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_of_Ohio">Governor of Ohio</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Governor of Ohio">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Even before McKinley completed his term in Congress, he met with a delegation of Ohioans urging him to run for governor. Governor <a href="/wiki/James_E._Campbell" title="James E. Campbell">James E. Campbell</a>, a Democrat, who had defeated <a href="/wiki/Joseph_B._Foraker" title="Joseph B. Foraker">Foraker</a> in 1889, was to seek re-election in 1891. The Ohio Republican party remained divided, but McKinley quietly arranged for Foraker to nominate him at the 1891 state Republican convention, which chose McKinley by acclamation. The former congressman spent much of the second half of 1891 campaigning against Campbell, beginning in his birthplace of Niles. Hanna, however, was little seen in the campaign; he spent much of his time raising funds for the election of legislators pledged to vote for Sherman in the 1892 senatorial election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner46_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner46-72"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan117.E2.80.93119_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan117.E2.80.93119-73"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Seventeenth_Amendment_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seventeenth_Amendment-74"><span>[</span>c<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley won the 1891 election by some 20,000 votes;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams50_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams50-75"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> the following January, Sherman, with considerable assistance from Hanna, turned back a challenge by Foraker to win the legislature’s vote for another term in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner86.E2.80.9387_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner86.E2.80.9387-76"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Blaine_breaks_out.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Blaine_breaks_out.png/220px-Blaine_breaks_out.png" width="220" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Blaine_breaks_out.png/330px-Blaine_breaks_out.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Blaine_breaks_out.png/440px-Blaine_breaks_out.png 2x" data-file-width="558" data-file-height="567" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Blaine_breaks_out.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Even after his final run for president in 1884, <a href="/wiki/James_G._Blaine" title="James G. Blaine">James G. Blaine</a> was still seen as a possible candidate for the Republican nomination. In this 1890 <i>Puck</i> cartoon, he is startling Reed and McKinley (right) as they make their plans for 1892.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ohio’s governor had relatively little power—for example, he could recommend legislation, but not veto it—but with Ohio a key <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">swing state</a>, its governor was a major figure in national politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams117_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams117-77"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Although McKinley believed that the health of the nation depended on that of business, he was evenhanded in dealing with labor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould7_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould7-78"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> He procured legislation that set up an arbitration board to settle work disputes and obtained passage of a law that fined employers who fired workers for belonging to a union.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams122_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams122-79"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">President Harrison</a> had proven unpopular; there were divisions even within the Republican party as the year 1892 began and Harrison began his re-election drive. Although no declared candidate opposed Harrison, many Republicans were ready to dump the President from the ticket if an alternative emerged. Among the possible candidates spoken of were McKinley, Reed, and the aging Blaine. Fearing that the Ohio governor would emerge as a candidate, Harrison’s managers arranged for McKinley to be permanent chairman of <a href="/wiki/1892_Republican_National_Convention" title="1892 Republican National Convention">the convention</a> in <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">Minneapolis</a>, requiring him to play a public, neutral role. Hanna established an unofficial McKinley headquarters near the convention hall, though no active effort was made to convert delegates to McKinley’s cause. McKinley objected to delegate votes being cast for him; nevertheless he finished third, behind the renominated Harrison, and behind Blaine, who had sent word he did not want to be considered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner92.E2.80.9396_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner92.E2.80.9396-80"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Although McKinley campaigned loyally for the Republican ticket, Harrison was defeated by former President Cleveland in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1892" title="United States presidential election, 1892">the November election</a>. In the wake of Cleveland’s victory, McKinley was seen by some as the likely Republican candidate in 1896.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan128.E2.80.93129_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan128.E2.80.93129-81"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Soon after Cleveland’s return to office, hard times struck the nation with the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1893" title="Panic of 1893">Panic of 1893</a>. A businessman in <a href="/wiki/Youngstown,_Ohio" title="Youngstown, Ohio">Youngstown</a>, Robert Walker, had lent money to McKinley in their younger days; in gratitude, McKinley had often guaranteed Walker’s borrowings for his business. The governor had never kept track of what he was signing; he believed Walker a sound businessman. In fact, Walker had deceived McKinley, telling him that new notes were actually renewals of matured ones. Walker was ruined by the recession; McKinley was called upon for repayment in February 1893. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan129.E2.80.93130_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan129.E2.80.93130-82"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> The total owed was over $100,000 and a despairing McKinley initially proposed to resign as governor and earn the money as an attorney.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan130.E2.80.93134_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan130.E2.80.93134-83"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Instead, McKinley’s wealthy supporters, including Hanna and Chicago publisher <a href="/wiki/H._H._Kohlsaat" title="H. H. Kohlsaat">H. H. Kohlsaat</a>, became trustees of a fund from which the notes would be paid. Both William and Ida McKinley placed their property in the hands of the fund’s trustees (who included Hanna and Kohlsaat), and the supporters raised and contributed a substantial sum of money. All of the couple’s property was returned to them by the end of 1893, and when McKinley, who had promised eventual repayment, asked for the list of contributors, it was refused him. Many people who had suffered in the hard times sympathized with McKinley, whose popularity grew.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan130.E2.80.93134_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan130.E2.80.93134-83"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> He was easily re-elected in November 1893, receiving the largest percentage of the vote of any Ohio governor since the Civil War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips67_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips67-84"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>McKinley campaigned widely for Republicans in the 1894 midterm congressional elections; many party candidates in districts where he spoke were successful. His political efforts in Ohio were rewarded with the election in November 1895 of a Republican successor as governor, <a href="/wiki/Asa_S._Bushnell_(Governor)" title="Asa S. Bushnell (Governor)">Asa Bushnell</a>, and a Republican legislature that elected Foraker to the Senate. McKinley supported Foraker for Senate and Bushnell (who was of Foraker’s faction) for governor; in return, the new senator-elect agreed to back McKinley’s presidential ambitions. With party peace in Ohio assured, McKinley turned to the national arena.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips69.E2.80.9370_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips69.E2.80.9370-85"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1896">Election of 1896</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Election of 1896">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1896" title="United States presidential election, 1896">United States presidential election, 1896</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Obtaining_the_nomination">Obtaining the nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Obtaining the nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mark_Hanna_by_WJ_Root,_1896_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Mark_Hanna_by_WJ_Root%2C_1896_cropped.jpg/220px-Mark_Hanna_by_WJ_Root%2C_1896_cropped.jpg" width="220" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Mark_Hanna_by_WJ_Root%2C_1896_cropped.jpg/330px-Mark_Hanna_by_WJ_Root%2C_1896_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Mark_Hanna_by_WJ_Root%2C_1896_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="335" data-file-height="369" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mark_Hanna_by_WJ_Root,_1896_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
McKinley’s close friend and adviser, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hanna" title="Mark Hanna">Mark Hanna</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It is unclear when William McKinley began to seriously prepare a run for president. As Phillips notes, “no documents, no diaries, no confidential letters to Mark Hanna (or anyone else) contain his secret hopes or veiled stratagems.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips61_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips61-86"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> From the beginning, McKinley’s preparations had the participation of Hanna, whose biographer William T. Horner noted, “what is certainly true is that in 1888 the two men began to develop a close working relationship that helped put McKinley in the White House.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner81_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner81-87"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> Sherman did not run for president again after 1888, and so Hanna could support McKinley’s ambitions for that office wholeheartedly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner92_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner92-88"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Backed by Hanna’s money and organizational skills, McKinley quietly built support for a presidential bid through 1895 and early 1896. When other contenders such as Speaker Reed and <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/William_B._Allison" title="William B. Allison">William B. Allison</a> sent agents outside their states to organize Republicans in support of their candidacies, they found that Hanna’s agents had preceded them. According to historian Stanley Jones in his study of the 1896 election,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Another feature common to the Reed and Allison campaigns was their failure to make headway against the tide which was running toward McKinley. In fact, both campaigns from the moment they were launched were in retreat. The calm confidence with which each candidate claimed the support of his own section [of the country] soon gave way to&#160;... bitter accusations that Hanna by winning support for McKinley in their sections had violated the rules of the game.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones103_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones103-89"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Hanna, on McKinley's behalf, met with the eastern Republican <a href="/wiki/Political_boss" title="Political boss">political bosses</a>, such as Senators <a href="/wiki/Thomas_C._Platt" title="Thomas C. Platt">Thomas Platt</a> of New York and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Quay" title="Matthew Quay">Matthew Quay</a> of Pennsylvania, who were willing to guarantee McKinley’s nomination in exchange for promises regarding patronage and offices. McKinley, however, was determined to obtain the nomination without making deals, and Hanna accepted that decision.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones105_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones105-90"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> Many of their early efforts were focused on the South; Hanna obtained a vacation home in southern Georgia where McKinley visited and met with Republican politicians from the region. McKinley needed 453½ delegate votes to gain the nomination; he gained nearly half that number from the South and <a href="/wiki/Border_states_(American_Civil_War)" title="Border states (American Civil War)">border states</a>. Platt lamented in his memoirs, “[Hanna] had the South practically solid before some of us awakened.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams57_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams57-91"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coronation_of_McKinley.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Coronation_of_McKinley.jpg/300px-Coronation_of_McKinley.jpg" width="300" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Coronation_of_McKinley.jpg/450px-Coronation_of_McKinley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Coronation_of_McKinley.jpg/600px-Coronation_of_McKinley.jpg 2x" data-file-width="848" data-file-height="553" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coronation_of_McKinley.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Louis Dalrymple cartoon from <a href="/wiki/Puck_(magazine)" title="Puck (magazine)"><i>Puck</i> magazine</a>, June 24, 1896, showing McKinley about to crown himself with the Republican nomination. The “priests” are Hanna (in green) and Congressman <a href="/wiki/Charles_H._Grosvenor" title="Charles H. Grosvenor">Charles H. Grosvenor</a> (red); <a href="/wiki/H._H._Kohlsaat" title="H. H. Kohlsaat">H. H. Kohlsaat</a> is the page holding the robe.</div>
</div>
</div>
