<p>The bosses still hoped to deny McKinley a first-ballot majority at <a href="/wiki/1896_Republican_National_Convention" title="1896 Republican National Convention">the convention</a> by boosting support for local <a href="/wiki/Favorite_son" title="Favorite son">favorite son</a> candidates such as Quay, New York Governor (and former vice president) <a href="/wiki/Levi_P._Morton" title="Levi P. Morton">Levi P. Morton</a>, and Illinois Senator <a href="/wiki/Shelby_Cullom" title="Shelby Cullom" class="mw-redirect">Shelby Cullom</a>. Delegate-rich Illinois proved a crucial battleground, as McKinley supporters, such as Chicago businessman (and future vice president) <a href="/wiki/Charles_G._Dawes" title="Charles G. Dawes">Charles G. Dawes</a>, sought to elect delegates pledged to vote for McKinley at the national convention in St. Louis. Cullom proved unable to stand against McKinley despite the support of local Republican machines; at the state convention at the end of April, McKinley completed a near-sweep of Illinois’ delegates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones119.E2.80.93125_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones119.E2.80.93125-92"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Former president Harrison had been deemed a possible contender if he entered the race; when Harrison made it known he would not seek a third nomination, the McKinley organization took control of Indiana with a speed Harrison privately found unseemly. Morton operatives who journeyed to Indiana sent word back that they had found the state alive for McKinley.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones117.E2.80.93119_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones117.E2.80.93119-93"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Wyoming Senator <a href="/wiki/Francis_Warren" title="Francis Warren" class="mw-redirect">Francis Warren</a> wrote, “The politicians are making a hard fight against him, but if the masses could speak, McKinley is the choice of at least 75% of the entire [body of] Republican voters in the Union”.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips71.E2.80.9372_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips71.E2.80.9372-94"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By the time the national convention began in <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a> on June 16, 1896, McKinley had an ample majority of delegates. The former governor, who remained in Canton, followed events at the convention closely by telephone, and was able to hear part of Foraker’s speech nominating him over the line. When Ohio was reached in the roll call of states, its votes gave McKinley the nomination, which he celebrated by hugging his wife and mother as his friends fled the house, anticipating the first of many crowds that gathered at the Republican candidate’s home. Thousands of partisans came from Canton and surrounding towns that evening to hear McKinley speak from his front porch. The convention nominated Republican National Committee vice chairman <a href="/wiki/Garret_Hobart" title="Garret Hobart">Garret Hobart</a> of New Jersey for vice president, a choice actually made, by most accounts, by Hanna. Hobart, a wealthy lawyer, businessman, and former state legislator, was not widely known, but as Hanna biographer <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Croly" title="Herbert Croly">Herbert Croly</a> pointed out, “if he did little to strengthen the ticket he did nothing to weaken it”.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner159.E2.80.93162_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner159.E2.80.93162-95"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams59_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams59-96"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_election_campaign">General election campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: General election campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">For additional information on the currency question, see <a href="/wiki/Cross_of_Gold_speech#Background" title="Cross of Gold speech">Cross of Gold speech#Background</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_straddle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A political cartoon. An imperially confident-looking man in an exaggerated military officer’s uniform is riding a plank of wood marked “Financial question,” which is balanced between two saw-horses. The man’s weight is bending the wood rather dramatically." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/McKinley_straddle.jpg/220px-McKinley_straddle.jpg" width="220" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/McKinley_straddle.jpg/330px-McKinley_straddle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/McKinley_straddle.jpg/440px-McKinley_straddle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="978" data-file-height="1172" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_straddle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Before the 1896 convention, McKinley tried to avoid coming down on one side or the other of the currency question. <a href="/wiki/William_Allen_Rogers" title="William Allen Rogers">William Allen Rogers</a>’s cartoon from <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%E2%80%99s_Weekly" title="Harper’s Weekly" class="mw-redirect">Harper’s Weekly</a></i>, June 1896, showing McKinley <a href="/wiki/Riding_the_rail" title="Riding the rail">riding the rail</a> of the currency question.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before the Republican convention, McKinley had been a “straddle bug” on the currency question, favoring moderate positions on silver such as accomplishing <a href="/wiki/Bimetallism" title="Bimetallism">bimetallism</a> by international agreement. In the final days before the convention, McKinley decided, after hearing from politicians and businessmen, that the platform should endorse the gold standard, though it should allow for <a href="/wiki/Bimetallism" title="Bimetallism">bimetallism</a> by international agreement. Adoption of the platform caused some western delegates, led by Colorado Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_M._Teller" title="Henry M. Teller">Henry M. Teller</a>, to walk out of the convention. However, compared with the Democrats, Republican divisions on the issue were small, especially as McKinley promised future concessions to silver advocates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips52.2C_81.E2.80.9382_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips52.2C_81.E2.80.9382-97"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECherny55.E2.80.9356_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECherny55.E2.80.9356-98"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones177_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones177-99"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The bad economic times had continued, and strengthened the hand of forces for <a href="/wiki/Free_silver" title="Free silver">free silver</a>. The issue bitterly divided the Democratic Party; President Cleveland firmly supported the gold standard, but an increasing number of rural Democrats wanted silver, especially in the South and West. The silverites took control of the <a href="/wiki/1896_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1896 Democratic National Convention">1896 Democratic National Convention</a> and chose <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a> for president; he had electrified the delegates with his <a href="/wiki/Cross_of_Gold_speech" title="Cross of Gold speech">Cross of Gold speech</a>. Bryan’s financial radicalism shocked bankers—they thought his inflationary program would bankrupt the railroads and ruin the economy. Hanna approached them for support for his strategy to win the election, and they gave $3.5 million for speakers and over 200&#160;million pamphlets advocating the Republican position on the money and tariff questions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould10.E2.80.9311_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould10.E2.80.9311-100"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech85.E2.80.9387_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech85.E2.80.9387-101"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">

<div>

</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan_presidential_campaign,_1896" title="William Jennings Bryan presidential campaign, 1896">Bryan’s campaign</a> had at most an estimated $500,000. With his eloquence and youthful energy his major assets in the race, Bryan decided on a <a href="/wiki/Whistle-stop" title="Whistle-stop" class="mw-redirect">whistle-stop</a> political tour by train on an unprecedented scale. Hanna urged McKinley to match Bryan’s tour with one of his own; the candidate declined on the grounds that the Democrat was a better stump speaker: “I might just as well set up a trapeze on my front lawn and compete with some professional athlete as go out speaking against Bryan. I have to <i>think</i> when I speak.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams130.E2.80.93131_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams130.E2.80.93131-102"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> Instead of going to the people, McKinley would remain at home in Canton and allow the people to come to him; according to historian R. Hal Williams in his book on the 1896 election, “it was, as it turned out, a brilliant strategy. McKinley’s ‘<a href="/wiki/Front_porch_campaign" title="Front porch campaign">Front Porch Campaign</a>’ became a legend in American political history.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams130.E2.80.93131_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams130.E2.80.93131-102"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flower_delegation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flower_delegation.jpg/220px-Flower_delegation.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flower_delegation.jpg/330px-Flower_delegation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flower_delegation.jpg/440px-Flower_delegation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="11720" data-file-height="8835" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flower_delegation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
William and Ida McKinley (to her husband’s right) pose with members of the “Flower Delegation” from <a href="/wiki/Oil_City,_Pennsylvania" title="Oil City, Pennsylvania">Oil City, Pennsylvania</a>, before the McKinley home. Although women could not vote in most states, they might influence male relatives and were encouraged to visit Canton.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>McKinley made himself available to the public every day except Sunday, receiving delegations from the front porch of his home. The railroads subsidized the visitors with low excursion rates—the pro-silver <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Plain_Dealer" title="Cleveland Plain Dealer" class="mw-redirect">Cleveland <i>Plain Dealer</i></a> disgustedly stated that going to Canton had been made “cheaper than staying at home”.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech88.E2.80.9389_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech88.E2.80.9389-103"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarpine52_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarpine52-104"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> Delegations marched through the streets from the railroad station to McKinley’s home on North Market Street. Once there, they crowded close to the front porch—from which they surreptitiously whittled souvenirs—as their spokesman addressed McKinley. The candidate then responded, speaking on campaign issues in a speech molded to suit the interest of the delegation. The speeches were carefully scripted to avoid extemporaneous remarks; even the spokesman’s remarks were approved by McKinley or a representative. This was done as the candidate feared an offhand comment by another that might rebound on him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech88.E2.80.9389_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech88.E2.80.9389-103"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams131.2C_226_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams131.2C_226-105"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones285_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones285-106"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Man_of_Mark.png" class="image"><img alt="A political cartoon. A closed fist protrudes from a jacket-sleeve covered in dollar signs; a cuff-link is marked “MARK $ HANNA”. The hand tightly grasps a chain from which hangs a tiny, sorry-looking figure marked “McKinley”. “A Man of Mark!” concludes the cartoon’s caption." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Man_of_Mark.png/170px-Man_of_Mark.png" width="170" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Man_of_Mark.png/255px-Man_of_Mark.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Man_of_Mark.png/340px-Man_of_Mark.png 2x" data-file-width="443" data-file-height="498" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Man_of_Mark.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
“A Man of Mark” 1896 <a href="/wiki/Homer_Davenport" title="Homer Davenport">Homer Davenport</a> cartoon of McKinley as Hanna’s creature, from <a href="/wiki/William_Randolph_Hearst" title="William Randolph Hearst">William Randolph Hearst</a>’s New York <i>Journal</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most Democratic newspapers refused to support Bryan, the major exception being the New York <i>Journal</i>, controlled by <a href="/wiki/William_Randolph_Hearst" title="William Randolph Hearst">William Randolph Hearst</a>, whose fortune was based on silver mines. In biased reporting and through the sharp cartoons of <a href="/wiki/Homer_Davenport" title="Homer Davenport">Homer Davenport</a>, Hanna was viciously characterized as a plutocrat, trampling on labor. McKinley was drawn as a child, easily controlled by big business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones176.E2.80.93177_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones176.E2.80.93177-107"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Even today, these depictions still color the images of Hanna and McKinley: one as a heartless businessman, the other as a creature of Hanna and others of his ilk.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner272.2C_318_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner272.2C_318-108"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Democrats had pamphlets too, though not as many. Jones analyzed how voters responded to the education campaigns of the two parties:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>For the people it was a campaign of study and analysis, of exhortation and conviction—a campaign of search for economic and political truth. Pamphlets tumbled from the presses, to be read, reread, studied, debated, to become guides to economic thought and political action. They were printed and distributed by the million&#160;... but the people hankered for more. Favorite pamphlets became dog-eared, grimy, fell apart as their owners laboriously restudied their arguments and quoted from them in public and private debate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones332_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones332-109"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The battleground proved to be the Midwest — the South and most of the West were conceded to Bryan — and the Democrat spent much of his time in those crucial states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech95_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech95-110"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKazin68_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKazin68-111"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> The Northeast was considered most likely safe for McKinley after the early-voting states of Maine and <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a> supported him in September.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips75_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips75-112"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> By then, it was clear that public support for silver had receded, and McKinley began to emphasize the tariff issue. By the end of September, the Republicans had discontinued printing material on the silver issue, and were entirely concentrating on the tariff question.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan184_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan184-113"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> On November 3, 1896, the voters had their say in most of the nation. McKinley won the entire Northeast and Midwest; he won 51% of the vote and an ample majority in the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a>. Bryan had concentrated entirely on the silver issue, and had not appealed to urban workers. Voters in cities supported McKinley; the only city outside the South of more than 100,000 population carried by Bryan was <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a>, Colorado.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKazin76.E2.80.9377_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKazin76.E2.80.9377-114"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The 1896 presidential election is often seen as a <a href="/wiki/Realigning_election" title="Realigning election">realigning election</a>, in which McKinley’s view of a stronger central government building American industry through protective tariffs and a dollar based on gold triumphed. The voting patterns established then displaced the near-deadlock the major parties had seen since the Civil War; the Republican dominance begun then would continue <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">until 1932</a>, another realigning election with the ascent of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Roosevelt" title="Franklin Roosevelt" class="mw-redirect">Franklin Roosevelt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliamsxiPhillips3.2C_77_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliamsxiPhillips3.2C_77-115"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Phillips argues that, with the possible exception of Iowa Senator Allison, McKinley was the only Republican who could have defeated Bryan—he theorized that eastern candidates such as Morton or Reed would have done badly against the Illinois-born Bryan in the crucial Midwest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips73.E2.80.9377_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips73.E2.80.9377-116"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the biographer, though Bryan was popular among rural voters, “McKinley appealed to a very different industrialized, urbanized America.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips77_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips77-117"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281897.E2.80.931901.29">Presidency (1897–1901)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Presidency (1897–1901)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inauguration_and_appointments">Inauguration and appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Inauguration and appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div>
</div>
</td>

<tr>
<td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg/220px-McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg/330px-McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg/440px-McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="8936" data-file-height="6880" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Melville_Fuller" title="Melville Fuller">Melville Fuller</a> swears in William McKinley as president; outgoing President <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> at right.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>McKinley was <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_William_McKinley" title="First inauguration of William McKinley">sworn in as president</a> on March 4, 1897, as his wife and mother looked on. The new President gave a lengthy inaugural address; he urged tariff reform, and stated that the currency issue would have to await tariff legislation. He warned against foreign interventions, “We want no wars of conquest. We must avoid the temptation of territorial aggression.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips207.E2.80.93208_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips207.E2.80.93208-118"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>McKinley’s most controversial Cabinet appointment was that of John Sherman as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould17.E2.80.9318_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould17.E2.80.9318-119"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> Sherman was not McKinley’s first choice for the position; he initially offered it to Senator Allison.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould17.E2.80.9318_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould17.E2.80.9318-119"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> One consideration in Senator Sherman’s appointment was to provide a place in the Senate for Hanna (who had turned down a Cabinet position as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postmaster_General" title="United States Postmaster General">Postmaster General</a>). As Sherman had served as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a> under Hayes, only the State position, the leading Cabinet post, was likely to entice him from the Senate. Sherman’s mental faculties were decaying even in 1896; this was widely spoken of in political circles, but McKinley did not believe the rumors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould17.E2.80.9318_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould17.E2.80.9318-119"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, McKinley sent his cousin, William McKinley Osborne, to have dinner with the 73-year-old senator; he reported back that Sherman seemed as lucid as ever.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan194.E2.80.93195.2C_285Leech152.E2.80.93153_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan194.E2.80.93195.2C_285Leech152.E2.80.93153-120"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley wrote once the appointment was announced, “the stories regarding Senator Sherman’s ‘mental decay’ are without foundation&#160;... When I saw him last I was convinced both of his perfect health, physically and mentally, and that the prospects of life were remarkably good.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan194.E2.80.93195.2C_285Leech152.E2.80.93153_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan194.E2.80.93195.2C_285Leech152.E2.80.93153-120"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After some difficulties, Ohio Governor Bushnell appointed Hanna to the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould19_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould19-121"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> Once in Cabinet office, Sherman’s mental incapacity became increasingly apparent. He was often bypassed by his first assistant, McKinley’s Canton crony Judge William Day, and by the second secretary, <a href="/wiki/Alvey_A._Adee" title="Alvey A. Adee">Alvey A. Adee</a>. Day, an Ohio lawyer unfamiliar with diplomacy, was often reticent in meetings; Adee was somewhat deaf. One diplomat characterized the arrangement, “the head of the department knew nothing, the first assistant said nothing, and the second assistant heard nothing”.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan194.E2.80.93195.2C_285Leech152.E2.80.93153_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan194.E2.80.93195.2C_285Leech152.E2.80.93153-120"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Maine Congressman <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Dingley_Jr." title="Nelson Dingley Jr." class="mw-redirect">Nelson Dingley Jr.</a> was McKinley’s choice for Secretary of the Treasury; he declined it, preferring to remain as chairman of the Ways and Means Committee. Charles Dawes, who had been Hanna's lieutenant in Chicago during the campaign, was considered for the Treasury post but by some accounts Dawes considered himself too young. Dawes eventually became <a href="/wiki/Comptroller_of_the_Currency" title="Comptroller of the Currency" class="mw-redirect">Comptroller of the Currency</a>; he recorded in his published diary that he had strongly urged McKinley to appoint as secretary the successful candidate, <a href="/wiki/Lyman_J._Gage" title="Lyman J. Gage">Lyman J. Gage</a>, president of the <a href="/wiki/First_Chicago_Bank" title="First Chicago Bank">First National Bank of Chicago</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Gold_Democrat" title="Gold Democrat" class="mw-redirect">Gold Democrat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould15Horner236.E2.80.93238_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould15Horner236.E2.80.93238-122"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Navy" title="United States Department of the Navy">Navy Department</a> was offered to former Massachusetts Congressman <a href="/wiki/John_Davis_Long" title="John Davis Long">John Davis Long</a>, an old friend from the House, on January 30, 1897.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould14_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould14-123"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Although McKinley was initially inclined to allow Long to choose his own assistant, there was considerable pressure on the President-elect to appoint <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>, head of the New York City Police Commission and a former state assemblyman. McKinley was reluctant, stating to one Roosevelt booster, “I want peace and I am told that your friend Theodore is always getting into rows with everybody.” Nevertheless, he made the appointment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan199.E2.80.93200_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan199.E2.80.93200-124"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to Sherman, McKinley made one other ill-advised Cabinet appointment,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips127_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips127-125"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> that of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a>, which fell to <a href="/wiki/Russell_A._Alger" title="Russell A. Alger">Russell A. Alger</a>, former general and <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> governor. Competent enough in peacetime, Alger proved inadequate once the conflict with Spain began. With the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War" title="United States Department of War">War Department</a> plagued by scandal, Alger resigned at McKinley's request in mid-1899.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould16.E2.80.9317.2C_174.E2.80.93176_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould16.E2.80.9317.2C_174.E2.80.93176-126"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> Vice President Hobart, as was customary at the time, was not invited to Cabinet meetings. However, he proved a valuable adviser both for McKinley and for his Cabinet members. The wealthy Vice President leased a residence close to the White House; the two families visited each other without formality, and the Vice President's wife, <a href="/wiki/Jennie_Tuttle_Hobart" title="Jennie Tuttle Hobart">Jennie Tuttle Hobart</a>, sometimes substituted as Executive Mansion hostess when Ida McKinley was unwell.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConnolly29.E2.80.9331_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConnolly29.E2.80.9331-127"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> For most of McKinley’s administration, <a href="/wiki/George_B._Cortelyou" title="George B. Cortelyou">George B. Cortelyou</a> served as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_to_the_President_of_the_United_States" title="Secretary to the President of the United States">his personal secretary</a>. Cortelyou, who served in three Cabinet positions under Theodore Roosevelt, became a combination <a href="/wiki/White_House_Press_Secretary" title="White House Press Secretary">press secretary</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_House_Chief_of_Staff" title="White House Chief of Staff">chief of staff</a> to McKinley.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner139.E2.80.93140.2C_240.E2.80.93241_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner139.E2.80.93140.2C_240.E2.80.93241-128"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_with_Spain">War with Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: War with Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a></div>
<p>For decades, rebels in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> had waged an intermittent campaign for freedom from <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spanish</a> colonial rule. By 1895, the conflict had expanded to a <a href="/wiki/Cuban_War_of_Independence" title="Cuban War of Independence">war for Cuban independence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould60_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould60-129"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> As war engulfed the island, Spanish reprisals against the rebels grew ever harsher. These included the removal of Cubans to <a href="/wiki/Internment_camps" title="Internment camps" class="mw-redirect">internment camps</a> near Spanish military bases, a strategy designed to make it hard for the rebels to receive support in the countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould61_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould61-130"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> American opinion favored the rebels, and McKinley shared in their outrage against Spanish policies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech148_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech148-131"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> As many of his countrymen called for war to liberate Cuba, McKinley favored a peaceful approach, hoping that through negotiation, Spain might be convinced to grant Cuba independence, or at least to allow the Cubans some measure of autonomy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould65.E2.80.9366_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould65.E2.80.9366-132"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> The United States and Spain began negotiations on the subject in 1897, but it became clear that Spain would never concede Cuban independence, while the rebels (and their American supporters) would never settle for anything less.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould68.E2.80.9370_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould68.E2.80.9370-133"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In January 1898, Spain promised some concessions to the rebels, but when American <a href="/wiki/Consul_(representative)" title="Consul (representative)">consul</a> <a href="/wiki/Fitzhugh_Lee" title="Fitzhugh Lee">Fitzhugh Lee</a> reported riots in <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a>, McKinley agreed to send the battleship <a href="/wiki/USS_Maine_(ACR-1)" title="USS Maine (ACR-1)">USS <i>Maine</i></a> there to protect American lives and property.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould71.E2.80.9372_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould71.E2.80.9372-134"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> On February 15, the <i>Maine</i> exploded and sank with 266 men killed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould74_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould74-135"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> Public opinion and <a href="/wiki/Propaganda_of_the_Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Propaganda of the Spanish–American War">the newspapers demanded war</a>, but McKinley insisted that a <a href="/wiki/Naval_Board_of_Inquiry" title="Naval Board of Inquiry">court of inquiry</a> first determine whether the explosion was accidental.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech171.E2.80.93172_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech171.E2.80.93172-136"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> Negotiations with Spain continued as the court considered the evidence, but on March 20, the court ruled that the <i>Maine</i> was blown up by an <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">underwater mine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech173Gould78.E2.80.9379_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech173Gould78.E2.80.9379-137"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> As pressure for war mounted in Congress, McKinley continued to negotiate for Cuban independence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould79.E2.80.9381_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould79.E2.80.9381-138"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Spain refused McKinley’s proposals, and on April 11, McKinley turned the matter over to Congress. He did not ask for war, but Congress declared war anyway on April 20, with the addition of the <a href="/wiki/Teller_Amendment" title="Teller Amendment">Teller Amendment</a>, which disavowed any intention of annexing Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould86.E2.80.9387_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould86.E2.80.9387-139"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Judge-2-6-1897.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Judge-2-6-1897.jpg/220px-Judge-2-6-1897.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Judge-2-6-1897.jpg/330px-Judge-2-6-1897.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Judge-2-6-1897.jpg/440px-Judge-2-6-1897.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="495" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Judge-2-6-1897.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Editorial cartoon intervention in Cuba. <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(name)" title="Columbia (name)">Columbia</a> (the <a href="/wiki/American_people" title="American people" class="mw-redirect">American people</a>) reaches out to help oppressed Cuba in 1897 while <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Sam" title="Uncle Sam">Uncle Sam</a> (the U.S. government) is blind to the crisis and will not use its powerful guns to help. <a href="/wiki/Judge_(magazine)" title="Judge (magazine)"><i>Judge</i> magazine</a>, February 6, 1897.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The expansion of the telegraph and the development of the telephone gave McKinley a greater control over the day-to-day management of the war than previous presidents had enjoyed, and he used the new technologies to direct the army’s and navy’s movements as far as he was able.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould91.E2.80.9393_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould91.E2.80.9393-140"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley found Alger inadequate as Secretary of War, and did not get along with the Army’s commanding general, <a href="/wiki/Nelson_A._Miles" title="Nelson A. Miles">Nelson A. Miles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould102.E2.80.93103_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould102.E2.80.93103-141"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> Bypassing them, he looked for strategic advice first from Miles’s predecessor, General <a href="/wiki/John_Schofield" title="John Schofield">John Schofield</a>, and later from <a href="/wiki/Adjutant_general#United_States" title="Adjutant general">Adjutant General</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clarke_Corbin" title="Henry Clarke Corbin" class="mw-redirect">Henry Clarke Corbin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould102.E2.80.93103_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould102.E2.80.93103-141"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> The war led to a change in McKinley's cabinet, as the President accepted Sherman’s resignation as Secretary of State; Day agreed to serve as Secretary until the war’s end.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould94Leech191_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould94Leech191-142"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Within a fortnight, the navy had its first victory when the <a href="/wiki/Asiatic_Squadron" title="Asiatic Squadron">Asiatic Squadron</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Commodore_(United_States)" title="Commodore (United States)">Commodore</a> <a href="/wiki/George_Dewey" title="George Dewey">George Dewey</a>, engaged the Spanish navy at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manila_Bay" title="Battle of Manila Bay">Battle of Manila Bay</a> in the Philippines, destroying the enemy force without the loss of a single American vessel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech203.E2.80.93207_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech203.E2.80.93207-143"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> Dewey’s overwhelming victory expanded the scope of the war from one centered in the Caribbean to one that would determine the fate of all of Spain's Pacific colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould96_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould96-144"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> The next month, he increased the number of <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Army_Corps_(Spanish-American_War)" title="Eighth Army Corps (Spanish-American War)" class="mw-redirect">troops sent to the Philippines</a> and granted the force’s commander, Major General <a href="/wiki/Wesley_Merritt" title="Wesley Merritt">Wesley Merritt</a>, the power to set up legal systems and raise taxes—necessities for a long occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould97.E2.80.9398_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould97.E2.80.9398-145"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> By the time the troops arrived in the Philippines at the end of June 1898, McKinley had decided that Spain would be required to surrender the archipelago to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould101_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould101-146"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> He professed to be open to all views on the subject; however, he believed that as the war progressed, the public would come to demand retention of the islands as a prize of war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan467.E2.80.93468_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan467.E2.80.93468-147"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, in the Caribbean theater, a large force of regulars and volunteers gathered near <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa, Florida</a>, for an invasion of Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech214.E2.80.93215_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech214.E2.80.93215-148"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> The army faced difficulties in supplying the rapidly expanding force even before they departed for Cuba, but by June, Corbin had made progress in resolving the problems.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould104.E2.80.93106_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould104.E2.80.93106-149"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> After lengthy delays, the army, led by Major General <a href="/wiki/William_Rufus_Shafter" title="William Rufus Shafter">William Rufus Shafter</a>, sailed from Florida on June 20, landing near <a href="/wiki/Santiago_de_Cuba" title="Santiago de Cuba">Santiago de Cuba</a> two days later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould107.E2.80.93109_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould107.E2.80.93109-150"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> Following a skirmish at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Las_Guasimas" title="Battle of Las Guasimas">Las Guasimas</a> on June 24, Shafter’s army engaged the Spanish forces on July 2 in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_San_Juan_Hill" title="Battle of San Juan Hill">Battle of San Juan Hill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech249.E2.80.93252_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech249.E2.80.93252-151"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> In an intense day-long battle, the American force was victorious, although both sides suffered heavy casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould109.E2.80.93110_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould109.E2.80.93110-152"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> The next day, the Spanish Caribbean squadron, which had been sheltering in Santiago’s harbor, broke for the open sea but was intercepted and destroyed by <a href="/wiki/Rear_admiral_(United_States)" title="Rear admiral (United States)">Rear Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/William_T._Sampson" title="William T. Sampson">William T. Sampson</a>’s North Atlantic Squadron in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Santiago_de_Cuba" title="Battle of Santiago de Cuba">largest naval battle of the war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech253.E2.80.93258_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech253.E2.80.93258-153"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> Shafter laid siege to the city of Santiago, which surrendered on July 17, placing Cuba under effective American control.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould110.E2.80.93112_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould110.E2.80.93112-154"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley and Miles also ordered an invasion of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, which met little resistance when it landed in July.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould110.E2.80.93112_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould110.E2.80.93112-154"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> The distance from Spain and the destruction of the Spanish navy made resupply impossible, and the Spanish government began to look for a way to end the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould112.E2.80.93113_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould112.E2.80.93113-155"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_and_territorial_gain">Peace and territorial gain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Peace and territorial gain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris,_1899.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG/220px-Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG/330px-Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG/440px-Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2752" data-file-height="2256" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris,_1899.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1898)" title="Treaty of Paris (1898)">Treaty of Paris</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On July 22, the Spanish authorized <a href="/wiki/Jules_Cambon" title="Jules Cambon">Jules Cambon</a>, the French Ambassador to the United States, to represent Spain in negotiating peace.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould112.E2.80.93113_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould112.E2.80.93113-155"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> The Spanish initially wished to restrict the discussion to Cuba, but were quickly forced to recognize that their other possessions would be claimed as spoils of war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould112.E2.80.93113_155-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould112.E2.80.93113-155"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley's cabinet agreed with him that Spain must leave Cuba and Puerto Rico, but they disagreed on the Philippines, with some wishing to annex the entire archipelago and some wishing only to retain a naval base in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould117_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould117-156"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> Although public sentiment seemed to favor annexation of the Philippines, several prominent political leaders, including Bryan, ex-President Cleveland, and the newly formed <a href="/wiki/American_Anti-Imperialist_League" title="American Anti-Imperialist League">American Anti-Imperialist League</a> made their opposition known.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould116_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould116-157"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>McKinley proposed to open negotiations with Spain on the basis of Cuban liberation and Puerto Rican annexation, with the final status of the Philippines subject to further discussion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould118.E2.80.93119_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould118.E2.80.93119-158"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> He stood firmly in that demand even as the military situation on Cuba began to deteriorate when the American army was struck with <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould118.E2.80.93119_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould118.E2.80.93119-158"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> Spain ultimately agreed to a ceasefire on those terms on August 12, and treaty negotiations began in Paris in September 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould120.E2.80.93121_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould120.E2.80.93121-159"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> The talks continued until December 18, when the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1898)" title="Treaty of Paris (1898)">Treaty of Paris</a> was signed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould142.E2.80.93143_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould142.E2.80.93143-160"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> The United States acquired Puerto Rico and the Philippines as well as the island of <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>, and Spain relinquished its claims to Cuba; in exchange, the United States agreed to pay Spain $20 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould142.E2.80.93143_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould142.E2.80.93143-160"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley had difficulty convincing the Senate to approve the treaty by the requisite two-thirds vote, but his lobbying, and that of Vice President Hobart, eventually saw success, as the Senate voted in favor on February 6, 1899, 57 to 27.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould144.E2.80.93150Morgan320_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould144.E2.80.93150Morgan320-161"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During the war, McKinley also pursued the annexation of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Hawaii" title="Republic of Hawaii">Republic of Hawaii</a>. The new republic, dominated by American interests, <a href="/wiki/Overthrow_of_the_Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Overthrow of the Kingdom of Hawaii">had seized power</a> from the royal government in 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould48_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould48-162"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> The lame-duck Harrison administration had submitted a treaty of annexation to the Senate; Cleveland, once he returned to office, had sent a special commission to the islands. After receiving the report, Cleveland withdrew the treaty, stating that the revolution did not reflect the will of Hawaiian citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan222_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan222-163"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, many Americans favored annexation, and the cause gained momentum as the United States became embroiled in war with Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould49.E2.80.9350_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould49.E2.80.9350-164"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley came to office as a supporter of annexation, and lobbied Congress to adopt his opinion, believing that to do nothing would invite a royalist counter-revolution or a Japanese takeover.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould49.E2.80.9350_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould49.E2.80.9350-164"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> Foreseeing difficulty in getting two-thirds of the Senate to approve a treaty of annexation, McKinley instead supported the effort of Democratic Representative <a href="/wiki/Francis_G._Newlands" title="Francis G. Newlands">Francis G. Newlands</a> of Nevada to accomplish the result by <a href="/wiki/Joint_resolution" title="Joint resolution">joint resolution</a> of both houses of Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould98.E2.80.9399_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould98.E2.80.9399-165"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> The resulting <a href="/wiki/Newlands_Resolution" title="Newlands Resolution">Newlands Resolution</a> passed both houses by wide margins, and McKinley signed it into law on July 8, 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould98.E2.80.9399_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould98.E2.80.9399-165"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley biographer H. Wayne Morgan notes, “McKinley was the guiding spirit behind the annexation of Hawaii, showing&#160;... a firmness in pursuing it”;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan223_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan223-166"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> the President told Cortelyou, “We need Hawaii just as much and a good deal more than we did California. It is <a href="/wiki/Manifest_destiny" title="Manifest destiny">manifest destiny</a>.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan225_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan225-167"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wake_Island" title="Wake Island">Wake Island</a>, an uninhabited <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atoll</a> between Hawaii and Guam, was claimed for the United States on July 12, 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCormick162_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCormick162-168"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expanding_influence_overseas">Expanding influence overseas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Expanding influence overseas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In acquiring Pacific possessions for the United States, McKinley expanded the nation’s ability to compete for trade in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCormick155_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCormick155-169"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> Even before peace negotiations began with Spain, McKinley asked Congress to set up a commission to examine trade opportunities in the region and espoused an “<a href="/wiki/Open_Door_Policy" title="Open Door Policy">Open Door Policy</a>”, in which all nations would freely trade with China and none would seek to violate that nation’s territorial integrity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould201_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould201-170"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/John_Hay" title="John Hay">John Hay</a> replaced Day as Secretary of State at the end of the war, he circulated notes to that effect to the European powers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould202.E2.80.93204_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould202.E2.80.93204-171"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> Great Britain favored the idea, but Russia opposed it; France, Germany, Italy and Japan agreed in principle, but only if all the other nations signed on.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould202.E2.80.93204_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould202.E2.80.93204-171"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Trade with China became imperiled shortly thereafter as the <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a> menaced foreigners and their property in China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould220.E2.80.93222_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould220.E2.80.93222-172"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> Americans and other westerners in <a href="/wiki/Peking" title="Peking" class="mw-redirect">Peking</a> were besieged and, in cooperation with other western powers, McKinley ordered 5000 troops to the city in June 1900 in the <a href="/wiki/China_Relief_Expedition" title="China Relief Expedition">China Relief Expedition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELafeber714_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELafeber714-173"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> The westerners were rescued the next month, but several Congressional Democrats objected to McKinley dispatching troops without consulting the legislature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould220.E2.80.93222_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould220.E2.80.93222-172"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley’s actions set a precedent that led to most of his successors exerting similar independent control over the military.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELafeber714_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELafeber714-173"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> After the rebellion ended, the United States reaffirmed its commitment to the Open Door policy, which became the basis of American policy toward China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould233_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould233-174"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Closer to home, McKinley and Hay engaged in negotiations with Britain over the possible construction of a canal across Central America. The <a href="/wiki/Clayton%E2%80%93Bulwer_Treaty" title="Clayton–Bulwer Treaty">Clayton–Bulwer Treaty</a>, which the two nations signed in 1850, prohibited either from establishing exclusive control over a canal there. The war had exposed the difficulty of maintaining a two-ocean navy without a connection closer than <a href="/wiki/Cape_Horn" title="Cape Horn">Cape Horn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould196.E2.80.93198_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould196.E2.80.93198-175"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> Now, with American business and military interests even more involved in Asia, a canal seemed more essential than ever, and McKinley pressed for a renegotiation of the treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould196.E2.80.93198_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould196.E2.80.93198-175"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> Hay and the British ambassador, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Pauncefote,_1st_Baron_Pauncefote" title="Julian Pauncefote, 1st Baron Pauncefote">Julian Pauncefote</a>, agreed that the United States could control a future canal, provided that it was open to all shipping and not fortified.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough256.E2.80.93259_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough256.E2.80.93259-176"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley was satisfied with the terms, but the Senate rejected them, demanding that the United States be allowed to fortify the canal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough256.E2.80.93259_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough256.E2.80.93259-176"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> Hay was embarrassed by the rebuff and offered his resignation, but McKinley refused it and ordered him to continue negotiations to achieve the Senate’s demands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough256.E2.80.93259_176-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough256.E2.80.93259-176"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> He was successful, and <a href="/wiki/Hay%E2%80%93Pauncefote_Treaty" title="Hay–Pauncefote Treaty">a new treaty</a> was drafted and approved, but not before McKinley’s assassination in 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough256.E2.80.93259_176-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough256.E2.80.93259-176"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tariffs_and_bimetallism">Tariffs and bimetallism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Tariffs and bimetallism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_Prosperity.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/McKinley_Prosperity.jpg/170px-McKinley_Prosperity.jpg" width="170" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/McKinley_Prosperity.jpg/255px-McKinley_Prosperity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/McKinley_Prosperity.jpg/340px-McKinley_Prosperity.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2786" data-file-height="4267" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_Prosperity.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1900 reelection poster celebrates McKinley standing tall on the gold standard with support from soldiers, sailors, businessmen, factory workers and professionals.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Two of the great issues of the day, tariff reform and free silver, became intertwined in 1897.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould40_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould40-177"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> Ways and Means chairman Dingley introduced a new tariff bill (later called the <a href="/wiki/Dingley_Act" title="Dingley Act">Dingley Act</a>) to revise the <a href="/wiki/Wilson%E2%80%93Gorman_Tariff_Act" title="Wilson–Gorman Tariff Act">Wilson–Gorman Tariff Act</a> of 1894.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould40_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould40-177"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley supported the bill, which increased tariffs on <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Luxury_good" title="Luxury good" class="mw-redirect">luxury goods</a>, but the proposed new rates alarmed the French, who exported many luxury items to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould40_177-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould40-177"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> The Dingley Act passed the House easily, but was delayed in the Senate as they assessed the French objections.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould41_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould41-178"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> French representatives offered to cooperate with the United States in developing an international agreement on <a href="/wiki/Bimetallism" title="Bimetallism">bimetallism</a> if the new tariff rates were reduced; this pleased silverite Republicans in the Senate, whose votes were necessary for passage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan211.E2.80.93212_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan211.E2.80.93212-179"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate amended the bill to allow limited reciprocity (giving France some possibility of relief), but did not reduce the rates on luxury goods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould42.E2.80.9344_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould42.E2.80.9344-180"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley signed the bill into law and agreed to begin negotiations on an international bimetallism standard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould44.E2.80.9345_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould44.E2.80.9345-181"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>American negotiators soon concluded a reciprocity treaty with France, and the two nations approached Britain to gauge British enthusiasm for bimetallism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould44.E2.80.9345_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould44.E2.80.9345-181"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> The Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a>, and his government showed some interest in the idea and told the American envoy, <a href="/wiki/Edward_O._Wolcott" title="Edward O. Wolcott">Edward O. Wolcott</a>, that he would be amenable to reopening the mints in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> to silver coinage if the <a href="/wiki/Viceroy%27s_Executive_Council" title="Viceroy's Executive Council">Viceroy's Executive Council</a> there agreed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould45.E2.80.9346_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould45.E2.80.9346-182"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> News of a possible departure from the gold standard stirred up immediate opposition from its partisans, and misgivings by the Indian administration led Britain to reject the proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould45.E2.80.9346_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould45.E2.80.9346-182"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> With the international effort a failure, McKinley turned away from silver coinage and embraced the gold standard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan217.E2.80.93218_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan217.E2.80.93218-183"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> Even without the agreement, agitation for free silver eased as prosperity began to return to the United States and gold from recent strikes in the <a href="/wiki/Klondike_Gold_Rush" title="Klondike Gold Rush">Yukon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australian_gold_rushes" title="Australian gold rushes">Australia</a> increased the monetary supply even without silver coinage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols586Gould46_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols586Gould46-184"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup> In the absence of international agreement, McKinley favored legislation to formally affirm the gold standard, but was initially deterred by the silver strength in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan218.E2.80.93219_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan218.E2.80.93219-185"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1900, with another campaign ahead and good economic conditions, McKinley urged Congress to pass such a law, and was able to sign the <a href="/wiki/Gold_Standard_Act" title="Gold Standard Act">Gold Standard Act</a> on March 14, 1900, using a gold pen to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould169.E2.80.93171_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould169.E2.80.93171-186"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Civil rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the wake of McKinley’s election in 1896, <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African Americans</a> were hopeful of progress towards equality. McKinley had spoken out against <a href="/wiki/Lynching" title="Lynching">lynching</a> while governor, and most African Americans who could vote supported him in 1896. McKinley’s priority, however, was in ending <a href="/wiki/Sectionalism" title="Sectionalism">sectionalism</a>, and they were disappointed by his policies and appointments. Although McKinley made some appointments of African Americans to low-level government posts, and received some praise for that, the appointments were less than they had received under previous Republican administrations. <a href="/wiki/Blanche_K._Bruce" title="Blanche K. Bruce" class="mw-redirect">Blanche K. Bruce</a>, an African American who during <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction Era of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Reconstruction</a> had served as senator from <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, received the post of register at the Treasury Department; this post was traditionally given to an African American by Republican presidents. McKinley appointed several black postmasters; however, when whites protested the appointment of Justin W. Lyons as postmaster of <a href="/wiki/Augusta,_Georgia" title="Augusta, Georgia">Augusta, Georgia</a>, McKinley asked Lyons to withdraw (he was subsequently given the post of Treasury register after Bruce’s death in 1898).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould153.E2.80.93154_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould153.E2.80.93154-187"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> The President did appoint <a href="/wiki/George_B._Jackson" title="George B. Jackson">George B. Jackson</a>, a former slave, to the post of customs collector in <a href="/wiki/Presidio,_Texas" title="Presidio, Texas">Presidio, Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouisiana_Historical_Assoc.2C_.27.27Cohen.27.27_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouisiana_Historical_Assoc.2C_.27.27Cohen.27.27-188"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup> However, African Americans in northern states felt that their contributions to McKinley's victory were overlooked; few were appointed to office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould153.E2.80.93154_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould153.E2.80.93154-187"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The administration’s response to racial violence was minimal, causing him to lose black support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould153.E2.80.93154_187-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould153.E2.80.93154-187"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> When black postmasters at <a href="/wiki/Hogansville,_Georgia" title="Hogansville, Georgia">Hogansville, Georgia</a> in 1897, and at <a href="/wiki/Lake_City,_South_Carolina" title="Lake City, South Carolina">Lake City, South Carolina</a> the following year, were assaulted, McKinley issued no statement of condemnation. Although black leaders criticized McKinley for inaction, supporters responded by saying there was little the president could do to intervene. Critics replied by saying that he could at least publicly condemn such events, as Harrison had done.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould155_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould155-189"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_at_Atlanta.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/McKinley_at_Atlanta.jpg/220px-McKinley_at_Atlanta.jpg" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/McKinley_at_Atlanta.jpg/330px-McKinley_at_Atlanta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/McKinley_at_Atlanta.jpg/440px-McKinley_at_Atlanta.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8850" data-file-height="7345" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_at_Atlanta.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
McKinley, (right of center) flanked by Georgia Governor <a href="/wiki/Allen_D._Candler" title="Allen D. Candler">Allen D. Candler</a> (front row to McKinley’s left) and Gen. <a href="/wiki/William_Rufus_Shafter" title="William Rufus Shafter">William Rufus Shafter</a>, reviewing the Atlanta Peace Jubilee parade, December 15, 1898.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to historian Clarance A. Bacote, “Before the Spanish–American War, the Negroes, in spite of some mistakes, regarded McKinley as the best friend they ever had.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBacote234_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBacote234-190"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> African Americans saw the onset of war in 1898 as an opportunity to display their patriotism; and black soldiers fought bravely at El Caney and San Juan Hill. African Americans in the peacetime Army had formed elite units; nevertheless they were harassed by whites as they traveled from the West to Tampa for embarkation to the war. Under pressure from black leaders, McKinley required the War Department to commission black officers above the rank of lieutenant. The heroism of the black troops did not still racial tensions in the South, as the second half of 1898 saw several outbreaks of racial violence; 11&#160;African Americans were killed <a href="/wiki/Wilmington_Insurrection_of_1898" title="Wilmington Insurrection of 1898" class="mw-redirect">in riots</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_North_Carolina" title="Wilmington, North Carolina">Wilmington, North Carolina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould156.E2.80.93157_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould156.E2.80.93157-191"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley toured the South in late 1898, hoping for sectional reconciliation. In addition to visiting <a href="/wiki/Tuskegee_Institute" title="Tuskegee Institute" class="mw-redirect">Tuskegee Institute</a> and black educator <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a>, he addressed the Georgia legislature, wearing a badge of gray, and visited Confederate memorials. In his tour of the South, McKinley did not mention the racial tensions or violence. Although the President received a rapturous reception from Southern whites, many African Americans, excluded from official welcoming committees, felt alienated by the President’s words and actions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould156.E2.80.93157_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould156.E2.80.93157-191"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBacote235.E2.80.93237Leech348_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBacote235.E2.80.93237Leech348-192"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to Gould and later biographer Phillips, given the political climate in the South, with white legislatures passing segregationist laws such as that upheld in <i><a href="/wiki/Plessy_v._Ferguson" title="Plessy v. Ferguson">Plessy v. Ferguson</a></i>, there was little McKinley could have done to improve race relations, and he did better than later presidents <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>, who doubted racial equality, and <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>, who supported segregation. However, Gould concluded, “McKinley lacked the vision to transcend the biases of his day and to point toward a better future for all Americans”.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould159.E2.80.93160Phillips149_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould159.E2.80.93160Phillips149-193"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_William_McKinley" title="List of federal judges appointed by William McKinley">List of federal judges appointed by William McKinley</a></div>
<p>After the retirement of Justice <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Johnson_Field" title="Stephen Johnson Field">Stephen Johnson Field</a>, McKinley appointed Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Joseph_McKenna" title="Joseph McKenna">Joseph McKenna</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> in December 1897.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould94_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould94-194"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> The appointment aroused some controversy as McKenna’s critics in the Senate said he was too closely associated with railroad interests and lacked the qualifications of a Supreme Court justice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESemonche374_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESemonche374-195"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite the objections, McKenna’s nomination was approved unanimously.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPratt29_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPratt29-196"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> McKenna responded to the criticism of his legal education by taking some courses at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Law_School" title="Columbia Law School">Columbia Law School</a> for several months before taking his seat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESemonche374_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESemonche374-195"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> Along with his Supreme Court appointment, McKinley appointed six judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Courts_of_Appeals" title="United States Courts of Appeals" class="mw-redirect">United States Courts of Appeals</a>, and 28 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_courts" title="United States district courts" class="mw-redirect">United States district courts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFederal_Judicial_Center_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFederal_Judicial_Center-197"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1900_election">1900 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: 1900 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg/220px-The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg/330px-The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg/440px-The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1058" data-file-height="787" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
McKinley ran on his record of prosperity and victory in 1900, winning easy re-election over <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">Bryan</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1900" title="United States presidential election, 1900">United States presidential election, 1900</a></div>
