<p>Republicans were generally successful in state and local elections around the country in 1899, and McKinley was optimistic about his chances at re-election in 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould207.E2.80.93208_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould207.E2.80.93208-198"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley’s popularity in his first term assured him of renomination for a second.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould213.E2.80.93214_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould213.E2.80.93214-199"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> The only question about the Republican ticket concerned the vice presidential nomination; McKinley needed a new running mate as Hobart had died in late 1899.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould215.E2.80.93217_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould215.E2.80.93217-200"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley initially favored <a href="/wiki/Elihu_Root" title="Elihu Root">Elihu Root</a>, who had succeeded Alger as Secretary of War, but McKinley decided that Root was doing too good a job at the War Department to move him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould215.E2.80.93217_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould215.E2.80.93217-200"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> He considered other prominent candidates, including Allison and <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Newton_Bliss" title="Cornelius Newton Bliss">Cornelius N. Bliss</a>, but none were as popular as the Republican party’s rising star, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips120.E2.80.93122_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips120.E2.80.93122-201"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup> After a stint as <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Assistant Secretary of the Navy">Assistant Secretary of the Navy</a>, Roosevelt had resigned and raised <a href="/wiki/Rough_Riders" title="Rough Riders">a cavalry regiment</a>; they fought bravely in Cuba, and Roosevelt returned home covered in glory. Elected governor of New York on a reform platform in 1898, Roosevelt had his eye on the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould215.E2.80.93217_200-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould215.E2.80.93217-200"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> Many supporters recommended him to McKinley for the second spot on the ticket, and Roosevelt believed it would be an excellent stepping stone to the presidency in 1904.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould215.E2.80.93217_200-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould215.E2.80.93217-200"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley remained uncommitted in public, but Hanna was firmly opposed to the New York governor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech531.E2.80.93533_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech531.E2.80.93533-202"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> The Ohio senator considered the New Yorker overly impulsive; his stance was undermined by the efforts of <a href="/wiki/Political_boss" title="Political boss">political boss</a> and New York Senator <a href="/wiki/Thomas_C._Platt" title="Thomas C. Platt">Thomas Platt</a>, who, disliking Roosevelt’s reform agenda, sought to sideline the governor by making him vice president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorner260.E2.80.93266_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorner260.E2.80.93266-203"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When the <a href="/wiki/1900_Republican_National_Convention" title="1900 Republican National Convention">Republican convention</a> began in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> that June, no vice presidential candidate had overwhelming support, but Roosevelt had the broadest range of support from around the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould215.E2.80.93217_200-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould215.E2.80.93217-200"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley affirmed that the choice belonged to the convention, not to him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould218_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould218-204"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> On June 21, McKinley was unanimously renominated and, with Hanna’s reluctant acquiescence, Roosevelt was nominated for vice president on the first ballot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech540.E2.80.93542_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech540.E2.80.93542-205"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1900_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1900 Democratic National Convention">Democratic convention</a> convened the next month in <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City,_Missouri" title="Kansas City, Missouri">Kansas City</a> and nominated William Jennings Bryan, setting up a rematch of the 1896 contest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould219.E2.80.93220_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould219.E2.80.93220-206"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The candidates were the same, but the issues of the campaign had shifted: free silver was still a question that animated many voters, but the Republicans focused on victory in war and prosperity at home as issues they believed favored their party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould226.E2.80.93227Leech543.E2.80.93544_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould226.E2.80.93227Leech543.E2.80.93544-207"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> Democrats knew the war had been popular, even if the imperialism issue was less sure, so they focused on the issue of trusts and corporate power, painting McKinley as the servant of capital and big business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould227.E2.80.93228Leech544.E2.80.93546_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould227.E2.80.93228Leech544.E2.80.93546-208"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> As in 1896, Bryan embarked on a speaking tour around the country while McKinley stayed at home, this time making only one speech, to accept his nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech549.E2.80.93557_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech549.E2.80.93557-209"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt emerged as the campaign’s primary speaker and Hanna helped the cause working to settle a coal miners strike in Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould228_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould228-210"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> Bryan’s campaigning failed to excite the voters as it had in 1896, and McKinley never doubted that he would be re-elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould229Leech558_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould229Leech558-211"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> On November 6, 1900, he was proven correct, winning the largest victory for any Republican since 1872.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech559_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech559-212"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup> Bryan carried only four states outside the <a href="/wiki/Solid_South" title="Solid South">solid South</a>, and McKinley even won Bryan’s home state of Nebraska.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech559_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech559-212"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_term">Second term</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Second term">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soon after <a href="/wiki/Second_inauguration_of_William_McKinley" title="Second inauguration of William McKinley">his second inauguration</a> on March 4, 1901, William and Ida McKinley undertook a six-week tour of the nation. Traveling mostly by rail, the McKinleys were to travel through the South to the Southwest, and then up the Pacific coast and east again, to conclude with a visit on June 13, 1901, to the <a href="/wiki/Pan-American_Exposition" title="Pan-American Exposition">Pan-American Exposition</a> in <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo, New York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller289.E2.80.93290_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller289.E2.80.93290-213"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the First Lady fell ill in California, causing her husband to limit his public events and cancel a series of speeches he had planned to give urging trade reciprocity. He also postponed the visit to the fair until September, planning a month in Washington and two in Canton before the Buffalo visit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould247.E2.80.93249_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould247.E2.80.93249-214"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination_and_death_throes">Assassination and death throes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Assassination and death throes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_William_McKinley" title="Assassination of William McKinley">Assassination of William McKinley</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_last_photo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/McKinley_last_photo.jpg/170px-McKinley_last_photo.jpg" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/McKinley_last_photo.jpg/255px-McKinley_last_photo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/McKinley_last_photo.jpg/340px-McKinley_last_photo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4217" data-file-height="5303" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_last_photo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
McKinley entering the Temple of Music on September 6, 1901, shortly before the shots were fired.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinleyAssassination.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/McKinleyAssassination.jpg/220px-McKinleyAssassination.jpg" width="220" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/McKinleyAssassination.jpg/330px-McKinleyAssassination.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/McKinleyAssassination.jpg/440px-McKinleyAssassination.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="639" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinleyAssassination.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist’s conception of the shooting of McKinley</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although McKinley enjoyed meeting the public, Cortelyou was concerned with his security due to recent assassinations by anarchists in Europe, such as the assassination of King <a href="/wiki/Umberto_I_of_Italy" title="Umberto I of Italy">Umberto I of Italy</a> the previous year, and twice tried to remove a public reception from the President’s rescheduled visit to the Exposition. McKinley refused, and Cortelyou arranged for additional security for the trip.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller294_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller294-215"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup> On September 5, the President delivered his address at the fairgrounds, before a crowd of some 50,000 people. In his final speech, McKinley urged reciprocity treaties with other nations to assure American manufacturers access to foreign markets. He intended the speech as a keynote to his plans for a second term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller298.E2.80.93300_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller298.E2.80.93300-216"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould250.E2.80.93251_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould250.E2.80.93251-217"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>One man in the crowd, <a href="/wiki/Leon_Czolgosz" title="Leon Czolgosz">Leon Czolgosz</a>, hoped to assassinate McKinley. He had managed to get close to the presidential podium, but did not fire, uncertain of hitting his target.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller298.E2.80.93300_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller298.E2.80.93300-216"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> Czolgosz, since hearing a speech by anarchist <a href="/wiki/Emma_Goldman" title="Emma Goldman">Emma Goldman</a> in Cleveland, had decided to do something heroic (in his own mind) for the cause. After his failure to get close enough on the fifth, Czolgosz waited the next day at the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Music" title="Temple of Music">Temple of Music</a> on the Exposition grounds, where the President was to meet the public. Czolgosz concealed his gun in a handkerchief, and, when he reached the head of the line, shot McKinley twice in the abdomen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller300.E2.80.93301_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller300.E2.80.93301-218"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>McKinley urged his aides to break the news gently to Ida, and to call off the mob that had set on Czolgosz—a request that may have saved his assassin’s life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller301.E2.80.93302_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller301.E2.80.93302-219"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> McKinley was taken to the Exposition aid station, where the doctor was unable to locate the second bullet. Although a primitive <a href="/wiki/X-ray_machine" title="X-ray machine" class="mw-redirect">X-ray machine</a> was being exhibited on the Exposition grounds, it was not used. McKinley was taken to the Milburn House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech596.E2.80.93597Miller312.E2.80.93315_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech596.E2.80.93597Miller312.E2.80.93315-220"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the days after the shooting McKinley appeared to improve. Doctors issued increasingly optimistic bulletins. Members of the Cabinet, who had rushed to Buffalo on hearing the news, dispersed; Vice President Roosevelt departed on a camping trip to the <a href="/wiki/Adirondacks" title="Adirondacks" class="mw-redirect">Adirondacks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller315.E2.80.93317Morgan401.E2.80.93402_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller315.E2.80.93317Morgan401.E2.80.93402-221"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup> Leech wrote,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is difficult to interpret the optimism with which the President’s physicians looked for his recovery. There was obviously the most serious danger that his wounds would become septic. In that case, he would almost certainly die, since drugs to control infection did not exist&#160;... [Prominent New York City physician] <a href="/wiki/Charles_McBurney_(surgeon)" title="Charles McBurney (surgeon)">Dr. McBurney</a> was by far the worst offender in showering sanguine assurances on the correspondents. As the only big-city surgeon on the case, he was eagerly questioned and quoted, and his rosy prognostications largely contributed to the delusion of the American public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech599_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech599-222"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Unknown to the doctors, the <a href="/wiki/Gangrene" title="Gangrene">gangrene</a> that would kill him was growing on the walls of his stomach, slowly poisoning his blood. On the morning of September 13, McKinley took a turn for the worse. Relatives and friends gathered around the death bed. At 2:15&#160;a.m. on September 14, President McKinley died. Theodore Roosevelt had rushed back and took the oath of office as president in Buffalo. Czolgosz, put on trial for murder nine days after McKinley’s death, was found guilty, sentenced to death on September 26, and executed by <a href="/wiki/Electric_chair" title="Electric chair">electric chair</a> on October 29, 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller321.E2.80.93330_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller321.E2.80.93330-223"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Funeral.2C_memorials.2C_and_legacy">Funeral, memorials, and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Funeral, memorials, and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funeral_and_resting_place">Funeral and resting place</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Funeral and resting place">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_Grave.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/McKinley_Grave.JPG/220px-McKinley_Grave.JPG" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/McKinley_Grave.JPG/330px-McKinley_Grave.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/McKinley_Grave.JPG/440px-McKinley_Grave.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2576" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_Grave.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
McKinley’s tomb in Canton</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_Memorial_Ohio_Statehouse.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/McKinley_Memorial_Ohio_Statehouse.JPG/220px-McKinley_Memorial_Ohio_Statehouse.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/McKinley_Memorial_Ohio_Statehouse.JPG/330px-McKinley_Memorial_Ohio_Statehouse.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/McKinley_Memorial_Ohio_Statehouse.JPG/440px-McKinley_Memorial_Ohio_Statehouse.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_Memorial_Ohio_Statehouse.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
McKinley memorial by <a href="/wiki/Hermon_MacNeil" title="Hermon MacNeil" class="mw-redirect">Hermon MacNeil</a> in front of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Statehouse" title="Ohio Statehouse">Ohio Statehouse</a>, Columbus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to Gould, “The nation experienced a wave of genuine grief at the news of McKinley’s passing.”<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould252_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould252-224"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> The stock market, faced with sudden uncertainty, suffered a steep decline—almost unnoticed in the mourning. The nation focused its attention on the casket that made its way by train, first to Washington, where it first lay in the <a href="/wiki/East_Room" title="East Room">East Room</a> of the Executive Mansion, and then in state in the Capitol, and then was taken to Canton.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan402.E2.80.93403_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan402.E2.80.93403-225"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup> A hundred thousand people passed by the open casket in the <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Rotunda" title="Capitol Rotunda" class="mw-redirect">Capitol Rotunda</a>, many having waited hours in the rain; in Canton, an equal number did the same at the Stark County Courthouse on September 18. The following day, a funeral service was held at the First Methodist Church; the casket was then sealed and taken to the McKinley house, where relatives paid their final respects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcElroy167_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcElroy167-226"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> It was then transported to the receiving vault at <a href="/wiki/West_Lawn_Cemetery" title="West Lawn Cemetery">West Lawn Cemetery</a> in Canton, to await the construction of the memorial to McKinley already being planned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan403_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan403-227"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There was a widespread expectation that Ida McKinley would not long survive her husband; one family friend stated, as William McKinley lay dying, that they should be prepared for a double funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller348_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller348-228"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup> This did not occur; the former first lady accompanied her husband on the funeral train. Leech noted “the circuitous journey was a cruel ordeal for the woman who huddled in a compartment of the funeral train, praying that the Lord would take her with her Dearest Love”.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech602_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech602-229"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup> She was thought too weak to attend the services in Washington or Canton, although she listened at the door to the service for her husband in her house on North Market Street. She remained in Canton for the remainder of her life, setting up a shrine in her house, and often visiting the receiving vault, until her death at age 59 on May 26, 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller348_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller348-228"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup> She died only months before the completion of <a href="/wiki/McKinley_National_Memorial" title="McKinley National Memorial">the large marble monument</a> to her husband in Canton, which was dedicated by President Roosevelt on September 30, 1907. William and Ida McKinley are interred there with their daughters, atop a hillside overlooking the city of Canton.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcElroy189.E2.80.93193Morgan406_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcElroy189.E2.80.93193Morgan406-230"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_memorials">Other memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Other memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:500_USD_note;_series_of_1934;_obverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg/300px-500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg" width="300" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg/450px-500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg/600px-500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1842" data-file-height="778" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:500_USD_note;_series_of_1934;_obverse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
McKinley on the <a href="/wiki/Large_denominations_of_United_States_currency" title="Large denominations of United States currency">$500 bill</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to the Canton site there are many memorials to McKinley. There is <a href="/wiki/National_McKinley_Birthplace_Memorial" title="National McKinley Birthplace Memorial">a monument at his birthplace</a> in Niles; 20&#160;Ohio schools bear his name.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcElroy189_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcElroy189-231"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup> There are several schools in the United States named <a href="/wiki/McKinley_School_(disambiguation)" title="McKinley School (disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect">McKinley School</a>. Nearly a million dollars was pledged by contributors or allocated from public funds for the construction of McKinley memorials in the year after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlcott388_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlcott388-232"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup> Phillips suggests the significant number of major memorials to McKinley in Ohio reflected the expectation among Ohioans in the years after McKinley's death that he would be ranked among the great presidents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips161_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips161-233"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> Statues to him may be found in more than a dozen states; his name has been bestowed on streets, civic organizations, and libraries. McKinley's name is also used in the large inner-city Honolulu, Hawaii high school, <a href="/wiki/President_William_McKinley_High_School" title="President William McKinley High School">President William McKinley High School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Denali" title="Denali">Denali</a>, in central <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, was named Mount McKinley in support of the then newly minted Republican nominee for President until The Alaska Board of Geographic Names changed the name of the mountain to Denali in 1975, which is how it was called by locals. The mountain's name was changed to Denali on the federal level by the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_the_Interior" title="Department of the Interior" class="mw-redirect">Department of the Interior</a> as a part of a visit to Alaska by President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> in August 2015;<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> its summit, at 20,310 feet (6,190&#160;m), is the highest point in North America. Similarly, until its name was changed to <a href="/wiki/Denali_National_Park" title="Denali National Park" class="mw-redirect">Denali National Park</a> on December 2, 1980, under congressional legislation signed by President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>, the park in which it is located was known as Mount McKinley National Park.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcElroy189_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcElroy189-231"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_historical_image">Legacy and historical image</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Legacy and historical image">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley1904-7.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/McKinley1904-7.jpg/220px-McKinley1904-7.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/McKinley1904-7.jpg/330px-McKinley1904-7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/McKinley1904-7.jpg/440px-McKinley1904-7.jpg 2x" data-file-width="746" data-file-height="525" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley1904-7.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Louisiana Purchase Exposition stamp (1904) honoring McKinley, who had signed a bill authorizing a subsidy for that upcoming event.</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harriet_Anderson_Stubbs_Murphy_-_William_McKinley_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Harriet_Anderson_Stubbs_Murphy_-_William_McKinley_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Harriet_Anderson_Stubbs_Murphy_-_William_McKinley_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="377" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Harriet_Anderson_Stubbs_Murphy_-_William_McKinley_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Harriet_Anderson_Stubbs_Murphy_-_William_McKinley_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Harriet_Anderson_Stubbs_Murphy_-_William_McKinley_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Harriet_Anderson_Stubbs_Murphy_-_William_McKinley_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3203" data-file-height="5490" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harriet_Anderson_Stubbs_Murphy_-_William_McKinley_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The official Presidential portrait of William McKinley</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>McKinley’s biographer, H. Wayne Morgan remarks that McKinley died the most beloved president in history.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan404_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan404-236"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the young, enthusiastic Roosevelt quickly captured public attention after his predecessor’s death. The new president made little effort to secure the trade reciprocity McKinley had intended to negotiate with other nations. Controversy and public interest surrounded Roosevelt throughout the seven and a half years of his presidency as memories of McKinley faded; by 1920, according to Gould, McKinley’s administration was deemed no more than “a mediocre prelude to the vigor and energy of Theodore Roosevelt’s”.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould252_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould252-224"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> Beginning in the 1950s, McKinley received more favorable evaluations; nevertheless, in surveys ranking American presidents, he has generally been placed near the middle, often trailing contemporaries such as Hayes and Cleveland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould252_224-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould252-224"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> Morgan suggests that this relatively low ranking is due to a perception among historians that while many decisions during McKinley’s presidency profoundly affected the nation’s future, he more followed public opinion than led it, and that McKinley’s standing has suffered from altered public expectations of the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan472_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan472-237"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There has been broad agreement among historians that McKinley’s election was at the time of a transition between two political eras, dubbed the <a href="/wiki/Third_Party_System" title="Third Party System">Third</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Party_System" title="Fourth Party System">Fourth Party Systems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENice448_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENice448-238"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup> Kenneth F. Warren emphasizes the national commitment to a pro-business, industrial, and modernizing program, represented by McKinley.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup> Historian Daniel P. Klinghard argued that McKinley’s personal control of the 1896 campaign gave him the opportunity to reshape the presidency—rather than simply follow the party platform—by representing himself as the voice of the people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlinghard736.E2.80.93760_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlinghard736.E2.80.93760-240"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup> However, more recently, as Republican political official <a href="/wiki/Karl_Rove" title="Karl Rove">Karl Rove</a> exalted McKinley as the agent of sweeping political realignment in the 2000s, some scholars, such as David Mayhew, questioned whether the 1896 election truly represented a realignment, thereby placing in issue whether McKinley deserves credit for it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERauchway242.E2.80.93244_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERauchway242.E2.80.93244-241"><span>[</span>238<span>]</span></a></sup> Historian Michael J. Korzi argued in 2005 that while it is tempting to see McKinley as the key figure in the transition from congressional domination of government to the modern, powerful president, this change was an incremental process through the late 19th and early 20th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKorzi281_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKorzi281-242"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Phillips writes that McKinley’s low rating is undeserved, and that he should be ranked just after the great presidents such as <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">Washington</a> and Lincoln. He pointed to McKinley’s success at building an electoral coalition that kept the Republicans mostly in power for a generation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips156.E2.80.93157_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips156.E2.80.93157-243"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup> Phillips believes that part of McKinley’s legacy is the men he included in his administration, who dominated the Republican Party for a quarter century after his death. These officials included Cortelyou, who served in three Cabinet positions under Roosevelt, and Dawes, who became vice president under <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Coolidge</a>. Other McKinley appointees who later became major figures include Day, who Roosevelt elevated to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> where he remained nearly twenty years, and <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a>, whom McKinley had made <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_the_Philippines" title="Governor-General of the Philippines">Governor-General of the Philippines</a> and who succeeded Roosevelt as president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips163.E2.80.93164_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips163.E2.80.93164-244"><span>[</span>241<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A controversial aspect of McKinley’s presidency is territorial expansion and the question of imperialism—with the exception of the Philippines, granted independence in 1946, the United States retains the territories taken under McKinley.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips154_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips154-245"><span>[</span>242<span>]</span></a></sup> The territorial expansion of 1898 is often seen by historians as the beginning of <a href="/wiki/American_imperialism" title="American imperialism">American empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips99_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips99-246"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup> Morgan sees that historical discussion as a subset of the debate over the rise of America as a world power; he expects the debate over McKinley’s actions to continue indefinitely without resolution, and notes that however one judges McKinley’s actions in American expansion, one of his motivations was to change the lives of Filipinos and Cubans for the better.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan468_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan468-247"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Morgan alludes to the rise of interest in McKinley as part of the debate over the more assertive American foreign policy of recent decades:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>McKinley was a major actor in some of the most important events in American history. His decisions shaped future policies and public attitudes. He usually rises in the estimation of scholars who study his life in detail. Even those who disagree with his policies and decisions see him as an active, responsible, informed participant in charge of decision making. His dignified demeanor and subtle operations keep him somewhat remote from public perception. But he is once again at the center of events, where he started.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan473_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan473-248"><span>[</span>245<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_cabinet">Administration and cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Administration and cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Medal_of_Honor-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Medal_of_Honor_24-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1896, some of McKinley’s comrades lobbied for him to be belatedly awarded the <a href="/wiki/Medal_of_Honor" title="Medal of Honor">Medal of Honor</a> for his bravery that day; Lieutenant General <a href="/wiki/Nelson_A._Miles" title="Nelson A. Miles">Nelson A. Miles</a> was inclined to grant McKinley the award, but when the then-President-elect heard about the effort, he declined it. See <a href="#CITEREFArmstrong">Armstrong</a>, pp.&#160;38–41; <a href="#CITEREFPhillips">Phillips</a>, p.&#160;21.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Twentieth_Amendment-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Twentieth_Amendment_52-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Until the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Twentieth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twentieth Amendment to the United States Constitution">20th Amendment</a> in 1933, the Constitution prescribed that Congress begin its regular sessions in early December. See <a href="#CITEREFUS_Senate.2C_Sessions_of_Congress">US Senate, <i>Sessions of Congress</i></a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Seventeenth_Amendment-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Seventeenth_Amendment_74-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Before the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Seventeenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Seventeenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Seventeenth Amendment to the United States Constitution</a> in 1913, senators were elected by state legislatures.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_McKinley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
